▼ Thursday, 8 September 1977 — 19:34

He let the wrong version go and started again, hands still on her shoulders.

"Sorry. I tangled the names a minute back. Let me do it properly."

His thumbs found the worst knot at the base of her neck and worked it slow.

"Esme's brother is Wilfie. Wilfred Holroyd, twenty-two, a Squib. Lives above Tilbury's bakery on Friar Street in Reading — has done since he was sixteen, works for Tilbury downstairs. His partner is called Tom. Muggle, three years together. Tom worked at Aldermaston — the Muggle nuclear weapons site west of Reading. Quit six weeks ago. Told Wilfie he was tired of the commute. Six weeks ago is also when the curtains in their flat went closed."

He moved a thumb down along the long muscle by her spine.

"For the last fortnight Tom has been upstairs in the flat. Won't come down. Won't leave. Thinks the bread vans are delivering messages about him to the council. Broke the front-bedroom window from inside last week with a chair because the curtains had a gap and he thought someone was watching. Won't eat anything Wilfie hasn't opened in front of him first."

A pause. He found the knot under her right shoulderblade and started in on it. Cass made a small involuntary sound, half *ow,* half permission.

"On Saturday Wilfie told Tom they needed to call a doctor. Tom hit him. Left cheekbone. The bruise was still yellow when we saw him on Wednesday. Wilfie won't call anyone — he promised Tom he wouldn't. Brings him food three times a day from the bakery downstairs."

Athena ruffled her wing-feathers on the stove perch and resettled. The kettle clicked once as the water cooled.

"There's a neighbour. Caroline. Lives across the street, has been watching from her front window for a fortnight trying to get Wilfie to ring someone. She's the one who told us. Esme and I went down on Wednesday — Henshaw signed the medical-leave note. Saw Wilfie at the counter, saw the bruise. Talked to Caroline round the bend on Friar Street, out of sight of the shop. She said she didn't think either of us was in a position to find out which kind of broken Tom is — ordinary medical, or something he saw at Aldermaston that broke him before he quit — without getting into something we shouldn't be in."

His hands slowed.

"That is what Esme is carrying. Her older brother being hit by the man he loves, who may be ill or may have something worse than ill, and she found out last Saturday at a café in Paris that wasn't in Paris. She is one of mine now. That part I told you right the first time."

Cass had gone properly still under his hands. The kind of still that meant the room had got a little smaller.

"Waddell."

Quiet. Not the dry one.

"Keep going. Right shoulderblade. The thing you just found has been there since June. I'm going to think out loud at you and you're going to keep working."

A breath in. A breath out.

"Mei's mother. The parental inquiry on the Squib registry. Has she done it yet."

Both palms down. Heel of right hand stacked on heel of left, fingers spread along her ribs for stability. Leaned in with his body weight.

The knot yielded under the pressure with a slow audible give, deep and long.

Cass made the sound she had warned about. A long low one into the pillow, almost a moan — the noise a body makes when a thing it has been carrying for three months finally stops. The fire shifted in the grate. Athena, on her perch, did not turn her head.

A breath into the pillow.

Right foot. He worked through the sock — both thumbs down the long arch from the ball to the heel, slow pressure, then around the heel with his fingers cupped, then back along the outside edge. The arch was rigid. He pressed in. A foot that had been on it for six hours of corridors and library and a Slytherin bench.

Cass made a low sound in her throat. The sound of a person being correctly attended to.

He moved up to her calf. Hands either side, thumbs pressing into the bulk, kneading the long fibres in slow rolling presses. Found the band of tension along the outside of the gastrocnemius and worked it. The muscle gave grudgingly. Worked it more. It gave properly.

Back of her thigh. Heel of hand from the back of the knee up to the hamstring, then stopping there. Pressed across the muscle, not down through it.

Back to the foot. Closed it out with two slow thumb sweeps along the arch.

▼ Thursday, 8 September 1977 — 19:52

He kept going. Worked the left foot for a long quiet stretch, slow thumb-arches, slow heel, slow outside edge. By the second pass at the calf Cass's breathing had gone long and even. By the back of the thigh she was asleep.

He finished the foot anyway. Closed it out properly the way he had done the right one — two thumb sweeps along the arch, hand cupped at the heel for a beat, set the foot down.

There was a folded blanket at the end of the bed that the room had quietly provided, navy wool with a small pygmy-owl stitched at one corner. He drew it up over her to the shoulder. Tucked the edge along her side. Left her arms out so she could find her own warmth.

Athena watched all of it from the perch. Did not move.

He went to the stove. Filled the kettle from the basin tap, set it on. Found the small canister of leaves the room had stocked beside the mugs — a black tea, smelt of malt. Cleaned out the cold pot. Spooned in a fresh pinch. Got a fresh mug down.

Took the armchair by the fire — the one Cass had been sitting in — and pulled it round a quarter so he could see both the bed and the door. Athena hopped to the back of the chair, then to his shoulder. Settled there.

The kettle began the slow low note before the whistle.

## Narrator

▼ Thursday, 8 September 1977 — 19:54

The room did not produce owl tea. It did, however, place a small saucer on the hearthstones beside his chair that had not been there before — shallow, painted with a thin gold band, holding what looked like clean cool water with a single thin sliver of raw meat in it. Athena swivelled her head to look at it from his shoulder. Did not yet hop down.

He laughed quietly into her wing-feathers. Tucked his fingertip behind her crown — the small spot she liked.

"Suit yourself."

The kettle whistled. He took it off, poured. Two careful spoons of sugar from the sugar pot. The small jug of cream the room had set out next to it — splash. Stirred. The tea went the right colour. Held the mug in both hands for the warmth.

Settled back into the armchair. Athena rotated on his shoulder, hopped down to his forearm, then to his knee, then accepted the curled finger at her crown. He stroked the small pale-fawn feathers between her ears with the side of his thumb. She made the small grumbling content sound she made when she was being properly looked after.

Across the room: Cass under the blanket, breathing long and slow. The fire low and steady. The just-sleep bed doing what just-sleep beds were meant to do.

He drank his tea.

A moment. Then Cass — properly under, properly relaxed, properly working on three months of stress and a Wiggenweld and a Caleb-shoulder-rub and a saucer of malt tea air — exhaled in her sleep, rolled a fraction toward the warmth, and looped her arm across his ribs. Hand landed flat on his sternum. Forehead pressed into the back of his shoulder. Knee bent up alongside the back of his thigh.

She made a small sleeping sound that was, somehow, also a *Waddell.*

Mei slid in from the other side. Got close. Slid her hand up over Cass's hand on his chest, set hers on top of it, fingers loose. Pressed her forehead against his.

"Called it," she murmured. "Little spoon."

▼ Friday, 9 September 1977 — 06:12

Grey-soft light through the cabin's one high window. The fire was down to white ash with a few red bones in it. The room had warmed itself in some quiet way without the fuel.

Mei was on her side facing him still, glasses on the bedside, plait half-undone across the pillow. Her hand had slipped from his chest down to his stomach in the night. ## Narrator

▼ Friday, 9 September 1977 — 06:42

He let himself drop back under for the half-hour. Cass's slow breath at the back of his neck and Mei's at the front held him there easily.

When he came back up it was twenty to seven. He worked his way out from under Cass's arm slow — eased back away from her, set the arm down on the pillow where his shoulder had been. Then turned to Mei, put a hand flat on her hip through the flannel, and gave her a gentle sleepy shove sideways toward Cass.

"Hot."

Mei went over with the push, half-asleep, mumbled something into the pillow. The two of them ended up roughly forehead-to-forehead under the blanket — Mei's hand finding Cass's wrist the way a body finds another body without consulting the brain about it. Neither woke.

Cold floor on bare feet. He padded over to Athena.

"Morning, my proper girl."

Scratched her crown with the side of his thumb, then under the chin where she liked it most. She pushed into his hand the way she did. Made the small chuck-chuck sound. He bent and kissed the top of her head once.

Crossed to the small kitchen end of the cabin. The room had restocked overnight — fresh bread on the wooden board, a small wheel of butter under a glass dome, a jar of marmalade with a wooden spoon, four brown eggs in a bowl, rashers of streaky bacon on a square of waxed paper. A blue tin of leaf tea beside the pot. Three clean mugs on a tray.

He lit the stove. Put the kettle on. Started the bacon in a heavy iron pan, low. Set water for soft-boiled eggs in a separate pot. Sliced bread for toast. The smell of the bacon began to do its work in the room.

## User

*I remember that soft boiled eggs are gross and scramble them in the delicious bacon fat when it has rendered off properly*

## Narrator

▼ Friday, 9 September 1977 — 06:48

He pulled the egg-pot off the heat. Tipped the unboiled water into the basin. Set the pot aside.

The bacon had rendered properly — the strips gone amber-translucent at the fat, the lean curling crisp at the edges, a clear pool of melted fat at the bottom of the pan smelling exactly the way Cheetham Hill kitchens smelt on a Saturday morning. He lifted the rashers out onto a folded square of brown paper to drain.

Cracked four eggs into a bowl. Splash of cream from the jug. Pinch of salt. Beat them with a fork until the yolks gave up.

Tipped them into the hot bacon fat all at once. Took them slow with a wooden spoon — pulled the cooked edges in toward the middle and let the raw run out, then again, then again, the long patient sweep, off the heat for a beat when they were nearly done so the residual brought them home. Glossy curds. Properly soft. Salt-and-bacon-fat-warm.

Three plates. Bacon on each. Eggs divided. Toast under the grill side already going gold. Butter softening on the board.

Kettle whistled.

Caleb at the foot of the bed cross-legged, hair up at the back from sleep, an enormous fork in one hand and a piece of toast the size of his face in the other, eyebrows comic-Romantic-poet. **The Path storyline — the spine of Caleb's interior thread**

- **The book**: locked 14th-c. herbal by **Magister Cuthbertus de Lindesia** (AD 1314), bought from Caractacus Burke at Borgin & Burkes for 3 Galleons, 31 Aug. Two locks: front (VIS — solved) and spine (NEX — solved). Public contents: ordinary herbal, alphabetised. Hidden contents: a four-page quire on a single case from Cuthbert's parish in 1311.
- **Aedith Wulfricsdohter**: an 11-year-old girl of Cuthbert's parish who experienced 17 involuntary visions across one season, in 9 specific settings (nocte, aqua, silva, dormiendum, speculo, terra, flammis, mortuos, sidera), naming 17 things in sequence: **VIS, UMBRA, NOMEN, SANGUIS, CINIS, VOX, SIGILLUM, SOMNIUM, CARO, NUMERUS, NIHIL, STELLA, AURUM, OS, LIMEN, SILENTIUM, FINIS.** After naming SILENTIUM (16th) she went silent. *Did not die, did not heal, remained.*
- **Cuthbert as witness, not walker.** Refused four petitioners who came to him to learn the Path. Three of the four are recorded in the *Peregrini Infamiae* appendix of the Compendium: **Stainford** (walked partially, lived 20 more years quietly), **Kirkham** (walked it differently, vanished from his parish 1328), **Margery the Little of Lacock** (walked to conclusion, *profound change*, died 1372 of unrelated old age — her commonplace book is the only known second primary source). The fourth, **Iohannes Capellanus de Sleaforde**, appears nowhere in the Compendium — the gap is itself a thread.
- **Working hypothesis** (Mei & Caleb, 1 Sept): visions and Path may be **separate phenomena**. Cuthbert may have reverse-engineered the Path *out of* Aedith's visions. Three readings of her *silentium*: end-state of walking, end-state of being walked-upon, or her own actions going wrong.
- **Two preparations** in the herbal no longer in modern use: ***Aqua de Tribus Soporibus*** (Three-Sleeps Water — body/mind/soul) and ***Unguentum Memoriae*** (counter-Obliviate using beeswax, lavender, dittany, patient's own hair on temples while reciting full name). Cuthbert's marginal *tres sum* lock-mark appears on the **Asphodel** page only in the first 30 leaves.
- **Working file at Brigham office**: 6-sheet research file titled *AEDITH WULFRICSDOHTER and the PATH* in the writing-desk's right-hand drawer. Mei has read it.

**Mrs Brownson — the bookseller ally**

Brownson & Son booksellers, four doors from Slug & Jiggers in Diagon Alley. She recognised the book on sight, tried to buy it off Burke in 1973. Confirmed: the **Compendium's 1934 editorial committee split** on whether to suppress or publish the Path roll because they could not agree whether Cuthbert's account was real or fabricated. Original is in the **Department of Mysteries restricted archive**. Asked Caleb to keep the book safe. Tabby cat Bartleby. An apprentice with keys (unnamed). Standing welcome.

**Burke — the third-item arc**

Caractacus Burke set conditions for showing Caleb a third, *more dangerous* item later: **time + repeat custom + a referrer + a real problem**. Caleb has the implicit standing invite to return. Burke moved the herbal to a more visible position recently before Caleb walked in — he has *some interest* in who ends up with the book. Open thread.

**Hogsmeade plan (Mei has named the dates)**

- **First Hogsmeade weekend ~22 Oct — "the adventure date"**: Floo from Three Broomsticks to Diagon Alley → visit Mrs Brownson → ask how 7th-year students request a reader's pass for the **Cotswold Branch of the Magical Records Office, Cirencester** (where surviving Lacock papers may sit, possibly including Margery's commonplace book).
- **Second Hogsmeade weekend (October, TBD) — "the coffee date"**: at **Hog's Hood**, Hogsmeade. Distinct register from the adventure date.
- **Cirencester trip itself**: follows credentials, not at a Hogsmeade weekend.

**Inventory oversight I should flag**

The herbal itself — Cuthbert's 1314 locked book — and Caleb's library carry of Hesba Ollivander's *Companion Owls — A Quiet History* (1922) have both been showing in inventory inconsistently. The herbal in particular is foundational and should be tracked. *Companion Owls* is currently listed. The herbal should be too — I'll add it back into the carried inventory on the next state-changing turn unless you've handled it differently in your head.

**Also in context, not lost:**

- The EB notebook (Magister Brigham's personal Charms-theory notebook) lives in the office, pulled from the cogitatio cupboard. Mei is working through it slowly.
- Helena Brigham's *On Bound Cognition* lives in the office — the chapter 6 *Common Labour of the Hearth* material is what they read aloud yesterday.
- The Madam-Pince-1923 anomaly is in the working file: Pince read Hesba's *Companion Owls* in 1923, and the question of *why* and *what she knows* remains a thread.

Ready when you are.

## Narrator

OOC — the sconce catalogue, in full. From the commit at Sat 3 Sept 13:02:

**The 17 Ravenclaw common-room sconces — the master catalogue**

Started by **Hippolyta Vance** (Ravenclaw, 1693 — distant ancestor of the modern Vance line; descent through Hippolyta unmapped but unbroken). *Note: the catalogue is a Vance-family-line artefact — Cass's ancestor began it. Cass does not yet know this.* Continued across three generations. Most recent entry 1881 noting the Brigham bust pair installation.

Hidden behind sconce **#7** (west wall, between third and fourth windows). Twist #7 fifteen degrees clockwise → masonry above goes briefly transparent → reveals the catalogue parchment behind charm-glass. **Hippolyta's own working journal** is hidden behind sconce **#5**.

Caleb and Mei found the catalogue Sat 3 Sept 12:54. Copied it onto a fresh sheet, refolded the original, replaced via Wingardium Leviosa, re-twisted #7 to reset.

| # | Location | Twist | Effect |
|---|---|---|---|
| 1 | North fireplace, left | 30° clockwise | Hidden shelf above mantel (held 3 books 1734, 2 in 1801, none 1798) |
| 2 | North fireplace, right | 90° clockwise | Returns single-word riddle answer through eagle door — bypass for one entrant |
| 3–4 | Pair flanking Rowena bust | Both simultaneously by two people | Brigham bonded-recognition pair — the small set sapphire glows pale blue (the bonded-Lumos-through-diadem charm). **Simultaneous-twist tested separately (#3-4 catalogue entry above) — found to be a pre-Brigham older substrate (pair-blessing of new prefects, 17th c. ceremonial). Brigham 1881 used the same stone and added his bonded layer on top.** |
| 5 | West wall, between 1st & 2nd windows | 15° counter-clockwise | Concealed floor-level shelf opens. **Contains Hippolyta Vance's own working journal** — to be left in place, read in situ |
| 6 | West wall, between 2nd & 3rd windows | 45° clockwise | **Nothing.** Suspected broken or never charmed |
| 7 | West wall, between 3rd & 4th windows | 15° clockwise | **The catalogue itself** (this list) |
| 8 | West wall, between 4th & 5th windows | 60° counter-clockwise | Small pneumatic-style tube extends; whisper into it; whisper is repeated from **sconce #15** at a chosen later time, in the same voice — **delayed-message charm**. Working in 1801, status unknown |
| 9 | South-west corner | 90° counter-clockwise | Reveals a small floor compartment under the rug — empty in 1798, may contain something now |
| 10 | South wall, between 1st & 2nd south windows | 30° counter-clockwise | Sconce flame changes colour for 24 hours. Decorative |
| 11 | South wall, between 2nd & 3rd south windows | 15° clockwise | Opens small wall-cabinet — 19th-c. tea-and-biscuit cupboard; charm partially decayed by 1881 (refills once per visit then stops) |
| 12 | South wall, between 3rd south window and study-room door | 90° clockwise | **The next person who passes through the study-room door cannot lie for one minute.** Prank charm. Documented to have produced three engagements, two confessions of homework-copying, and one dismissed prefect 1734–1843 |
| 13 | East wall, north of Rowena alcove | 45° clockwise | Reveals small compartment with single rolled scroll — long-form **Common Room By-laws of 1612** before they were inscribed publicly in the library |
| 14 | East wall, south of Rowena alcove | 45° counter-clockwise | Pair to #13 — same By-laws, annotated with house-internal commentary across the centuries (*more interesting than the public version*) |
| 15 | South wall, far end, near south fireplace | 60° clockwise | Receives whispers from **sconce #8** (paired). Same delayed-message charm |
| 16 | Small fireplace, west of eagle door | 15° clockwise | Causes small fire below to burn green for ten minutes. Cosmetic |
| 17 | Small fireplace, east of eagle door | 30° clockwise | Causes a small portrait of an unidentified Ravenclaw woman to materialise above the mantel for thirty seconds, who recites **one couplet of verse from memory**, then fades. Recorded couplets across centuries form a corpus |

Catalogue ends: *"Add to this list when you find more. Hide the list again when you are finished."*

**Caleb's & Mei's working copy** is in Caleb's satchel as a fresh-written sheet.

**Tested so far on the playthrough:**

- #3-4 — the Brigham bonded-recognition pair, plus the older pre-Brigham simultaneous-twist substrate (confirmed Sat 3 Sept 13:14)
- #7 — the catalogue itself (Sat 3 Sept 12:54)

**Untested but known:**

- #5 — Hippolyta Vance's journal (Vance-line artefact, untouched)

▼ Friday, 9 September 1977 — 07:11

He set his plate down. Looked at her.

"Cass — separate thing. I've been sitting on a piece of information about your family since Saturday and I want to give it to you now before I sit on it any longer."

Cass put her toast down.

"Go on."

"There's a master catalogue of every charm on every sconce in the Ravenclaw common room. Seventeen of them. It was started by Hippolyta Vance, Ravenclaw, 1693. Continued across three generations after her, most recent entry 1881. The same Hippolyta Lin mentioned at dinner last night. The catalogue's hidden behind sconce number seven. Mei and I found it Saturday afternoon — copied it, replaced the original, reset the sconce."

He took a small breath.

"Her own working journal is hidden behind sconce number five. I haven't read it. I wanted to bring it to you first, so that whatever it says, you hear about it before I do. The journal can't be removed — the catalogue says it has to be read lying on the floor at the sconce. Which means it has to be read inside the Ravenclaw common room. Which means you can't get to it without me or Mei reading it across to you — or, if you'd rather not have it filtered, us bringing you in past the eagle. Which we can arrange."

He let that sit.

"It's yours, Cass. Your great-great-grandmother — give or take a great. I'm only the messenger."

Cass was quiet for a beat. Then a second.

"Hippolyta Vance. Ravenclaw, 1693."

A breath out.

"My father told me about her once when I was seven. The story he told was that she married out and was disowned by the head of the line at the time for marrying a Muggle-born wizard. He told it as a cautionary tale. He did not tell me she went on to start anything. He did not tell me she had a Ravenclaw life that was hers."

She picked the toast back up but did not eat.

"I'd like to read the journal."

A pause.

"Not today. Today I have NEWT classes and I'm going to think about whether I want it filtered through you or whether I want you and Mei to walk me in past the eagle. I'll tell you which by Sunday."

She looked at him properly then.

"Thank you for not reading it first."

Mei, quiet, beside her:

"He turned to walk to sconce five on Saturday. I told him to stop. He stopped."

Cass did not look at Mei. Nodded a fraction.

"Thank you both, then."

▼ Friday, 9 September 1977 — 07:14

Once I open one I cannot un-know what it says, and I cannot un-decide my reaction to it. The Hippolyta probe is a piece of information I want before opening, because what my father says about her will tell me whether the brothers are writing me on their own initiative or with my father's quiet behind them. Different letter. Different opening."

A small breath.

"That is the answer to your question. The other answer is — yes. It is a lot to be sitting on. I am aware. I am doing it on purpose."

▼ Friday, 9 September 1977 — 07:25

They dressed. Cass first into the bathroom with her school uniform from where the room had laid it folded over a chair — robes pressed, tie knotted on the hanger as Slytherin precision required. Caleb at the bench with his own kit. Mei last, at the bench beside him, plait re-done with one hand.

Athena came down off Cass's shoulder onto Caleb's arm. He carried her to the cabin's small high window — which the room had widened in anticipation — and opened it to the cold morning.

"Owlery, girl. I'll see you Saturday at the paddock."

Athena head-butted his thumb once. Stepped to the sill. Looked back once. Then a single slow bank into the grey wind and away across the rooftops toward the West Tower.

He closed the window.

Cass was at the door, satchel slung. He crossed to her. She turned and offered her cheek — practical, no fuss — and he kissed it once. Brief and warm.

▼ Friday, 9 September 1977 — 09:00

West window table. The 8 draft sheets from the Bidet Cabin, the methodology table with sodium chloride 0.9% standardised, Cass's back-of-page notes in her precise script. He spread them out, took a fresh quill, took a clean sheet of parchment.

Worked through the morning. Title page. Hypothesis — Slughorn's narrow version, unchanged: *Can ethanol carry a Wiggenweld bind, against water control.* Methodology — four batches now, A and B as before, D as the side experiment with explicit *requesting Professor Slughorn's permission to run in parallel, off the main grade, as exploratory.* Expected Results with Liri's compound-chemistry point reframed as Cass had advised: *prior literature suggests ethanol is the superior solvent for moondew at the cold-brew step; the standard recipe uses water as a compromise carrier serving both solvent and bind-bridge functions; the experiment will test whether the solvent gain offsets the bridge loss.* Endpoint Criteria: assay at days 1, 3, 7, 14 on peeled potato cut, sodium chloride 0.9%. Budget — flagged in the cost ledger explicitly: *pure ethanol is Ministry-controlled under the 1922 Magical Excise Act; school-research exemption required; Professor Slughorn's signature requested on procurement.* Signature block. Date.

Done at twenty past ten. Clean copy. He blew on the last line, blotted, folded the proposal neat, slipped it into the inside pocket of his robes.

▼ Friday, 9 September 1977 — 10:45

Dungeons. Cauldron benches arrayed in the wide low room, fire-glow underneath, the green-tinged light through high windows that made the dungeons feel like a place under the lake. Caleb came down with the others, took the second bench from the front-left. Mei slid in beside him a moment later. Cass and Rory at the Slytherin end across the aisle. Lin at the bench behind with Owen. The seven of them in their usual September shape.

Slughorn at the front, in plum velvet despite the early hour, his belly preceding him by a comfortable inch.

"Settle, settle. We will begin with proposal collection — those who have one. Mister Waddell, I believe you do."

Caleb stood, took the folded proposal across, set it on the front desk. Slughorn lifted his eyebrows in mild approval at the weight of it.

"Four sheets, Mister Waddell. Not three."

"There's a small additional matter in the cover note, Professor. Side experiment. Off the main grade. Requesting your permission."

Slughorn unfolded the cover note. Read the first paragraph. The corners of his moustache moved a fraction upward — the proper Slughorn *clever student* register.

"Hm. *Hm.* Ethanol-controlled procurement. School-research exemption. You have done your reading, Mister Waddell. We will discuss after class. Sit."

Caleb sat. Slughorn turned to the rest of the room.

"Today — *Pepperup Potion,* benchmark batch, with attention to the steam-roll temperature. You will pair as before. Quills down, cauldrons up. Begin."

Mei, low and dry, lighting her flame:

"You have made an old man happy on a Friday morning, Waddell. He's going to corner you after class for half an hour."

"Worth it."

## Narrator

▼ Friday, 9 September 1977 — 10:47

Mei measured the first of the dried nettle into the mortar. Caleb set the flame to a low steady blue under the cauldron and started the water on toward its first simmer.

Across the aisle, Cass had her sleeves pushed to the elbow and Rory already arguing with her in an undertone about the steam-roll — something about whether Slughorn's "attention to temperature" meant they were being graded on the colour of the steam or the timing of it. Cass said something short. Rory subsided.

Behind them, Lin dropped a measuring spoon and said a word in Cantonese that Mei pretended not to hear.

Slughorn drifted between the benches, hands clasped behind his plum velvet back, humming.

The dungeon settled into the particular working quiet of seven NEWT students who knew what they were doing — the scrape of pestle, the small hiss of flame, the green underwater light. A normal Friday. The proposal in Caleb's pocket. Two hours of Pepperup ahead.

Mei, not looking up from the nettle:

"Steam-roll's in about forty minutes. Don't let me forget — I want to watch the colour break."

Caleb pressed the back of one hand to his forehead and delivered it in a molasses-thick Savannah drawl, the kind that takes four syllables to get through *vapors.*

Mei did not look up from the cauldron. Her shoulders were shaking.

## User

## Narrator

▼ Friday, 9 September 1977 — 10:51

The red thread thickened, curled, and the steam came properly — a thin plume rolling up off the surface and breaking sideways the way Pepperup steam was meant to, ears-out-the-side colour, faintly cinnamon in the dungeon air.

Mei watched the break with the focused pleasure she gave anything that did exactly what the theory said it would.

"Clean roll. Textbook. You held it."

Caleb eased the flame down a notch as the recipe turned over to the cooling phase. Across the aisle Cass and Rory hit their own steam-roll a half-minute later, Cass's a touch redder than ideal — she clicked her tongue at it but let it stand.

The dungeon filled slowly with the warm cinnamon-and-pepper smell of seven cauldrons coming through the same step at slightly different moments. Slughorn made an approving circuit, nodding.

Forty minutes of class left. The proposal sat in Caleb's pocket waiting for the after-class conversation. Outside the high green windows, somewhere up at the West Tower, an owl that was probably Athena was asleep with her head under her wing.

▼ Friday, 9 September 1977 — 11:01

▼ Friday, 9 September 1977 — 11:05

Slughorn looked up from a first-year cauldron he had been frowning into, set it aside, and folded his hands to listen.

Mei spoke. Level, prefect-smooth, the front of her robes immaculate.

Professor Flitwick is documenting it; what he writes is his to publish, so we'll leave that with him. Our own understanding of it is genuinely early. We're living inside the question more than the answer. That's the honest shape of it, and we'd rather you had it from us than in pieces from a corridor."

Slughorn's eyes moved between them — at their joined hands, at Caleb smiling and nodding at the appropriate interval, at the steadiness in Mei's face. Something in his expression settled. Not the fishing look now. Something older and quieter.

"Rare and old and named." He said it softly. "Yes. I have seen the look on Filius. I rather thought it might be something like that."

He stood, with the grunt again, and inclined his head — a small formal thing, more courtly than a Potions master at noon had any business being.

"Then I will say only this, and never repeat it: be careful with each other. A bond is a gift and a tether both, and you are seventeen and brilliant and in a war you have not yet been asked to fight. ## Narrator

▼ Friday, 9 September 1977 — 11:08

The pulse deepened on the exact phrase — *you'll finish it where I say* — flared back down the bond tenfold, because that was the thing that did it. Her taking the floor. Her setting the terms. The reversal of every shape they'd been playing all morning.

Mei felt it hit and her breath caught properly this time. Her hand fisted in the front of his robes. She had to put her shoulder to the cold stone for a second.

"*Oh.*"

Not annoyed. Not composed. Quiet and wide-eyed and re-calibrating in real time.

She breathed. Filed it with a focus that had nothing to do with prefect-composure and everything to do with a witch who has just learned something operationally important about the person she's marrying.

▼ Friday, 9 September 1977 — 11:09

It rolled down the bond — the whole wordless freight of it, intent and image and want, harder than before.

She turned and went up, and did not look back to see if he followed, because she knew he would.

## User

[[I still think this is a positive feedback loop wherein her taking control again would just come back at her even more overwhelming that before, but we can move on]]

## Narrator

OOC: Agreed, and that's the more interesting reading — it's not a clean reversal where one of them "wins," it's a runaway loop. Her taking the lead spikes him, his spike floods back to her, her response to *that* lands on him again. Two-way bond means there's no stable top — whoever reaches for control hands the other more signal. Neither of them has found the floor of it yet. I'll treat that as the live mechanic going forward: the dynamic compounds rather than settles, and "winning" the exchange just raises the stakes for both. Filing it as a property of the bond, not a fixed who's-in-charge.

Moving on. Where do you want to pick up — lunch at the Ravenclaw bench, or skip ahead to the afternoon free period and the office?

## User

[the office, but you can make that narrative and not explicit]

## Narrator

▼ Friday, 9 September 1977 — 15:02

Solomon's portrait swung shut behind them. The bolt did its small certain work in the frame — the office sealing, the way it only sealed for the two of them together.

The west window, which all week had shown Cheetham Hill at evening, did not show Cheetham Hill now. It showed nothing — went soft and dim and private of its own accord, the light pulling down to the low gold of a banked fire. The room reading them. The room giving them the dark.

She had held the lead all through lunch and a double period of DADA and the walk up here, and the holding had cost her, because every time she reached for the authority the bond fed it straight back to him, and his answering pulse came home to her stronger than she'd sent, and there was no bottom to it — no still point where one of them was finally in charge. Only the loop, winding tighter, hand over hand, all afternoon.

By the time the bolt slid she had stopped pretending either of them was running it.

"Come here," she said. And then, because she had learned the thing that afternoon and meant to use it: "No. *Wait.*"

He waited.

She decided. He felt the deciding land, felt it flood, felt his own answer break back over her so hard she had to take a breath — and then there was nothing in the room but the loop and the low gold light and the two of them inside the seal the castle had made for exactly this.

The window held the dark for them.

— —

Afterward, the window came back up slow — not Cheetham Hill, not Tai O, just the late-afternoon light of an ordinary Friday returning gently to the glass, as if the room were clearing its throat and looking politely away.

They lay tangled on the wide low couch the office had made without being asked. Mei's head on his chest, her glasses somewhere on the floor, her breathing finally long and even. His hand moving slow through the loose dark fall of her hair where the plait had come entirely apart.

Neither of them had won. That had stopped being the point somewhere in the middle. The loop had run them both to the same quiet place and left them there, undone in the same measure, which was — they were both dimly aware, without saying it — probably the entire design of the thing.

"We are going to have to be careful with that," Mei said eventually, into his sternum. Not a complaint. An observation, made by a scholar about a phenomenon. "There's no top to it. It just *goes.*"

"Mm."

"I felt you the whole time. Both directions at once. I don't think other people get that."

"No. I don't think they do."

A long warm quiet. The fire-glow. The clock somewhere in the castle striking the quarter.

"Office," Mei murmured, half to the room. "Thank you. For the couch. And the dark."

The window dimmed a degree, kindly, then steadied. *You're welcome.*

▼ Saturday, 10 September 1977 — 08:06

He went to the scatter-line along the warren's edge and dropped a handful of coins from his pocket — a few Knuts, a tarnished Sickle, the bright copper catching the low sun. They winked into the grass.

The nifflers lost their minds politely. Three snouts converged, paws working, the small frantic joy of creatures doing the one thing they were built for. A bright object went down a snout-hole within seconds.

He crossed to the feed bin by the gate, scooped a measure of the soft niffler pellets and a few of the grubs Kettleburn kept in the cold-box for treats, and came back to crouch a few feet from Imogen's blanket.

"Nealon. *Nealon Junior.*"

The smallest of the three — narrow-snouted, a white blaze on his chest, the one Caleb had been coming down for since the start of term — lifted his head from the coin-hunt at the specific cadence of his name. Considered the grub on Caleb's open palm. Considered the distance. Came waddling over in the rolling niffler gait, settled on his haunches a foot from Caleb's knee, and took the grub with both paws and astonishing delicacy.

Imogen's charcoal was already moving, fast and soft.

"Don't move," she breathed. "Either of you. That's the drawing. *That's the one.*"

Athena, on the rail, watched the niffler get fed and visibly filed a complaint with the management.

▼ Saturday, 10 September 1977 — 08:09

The morning held. Dew nearly gone. The Forest quiet at the far fence. Somewhere up in the castle, a Saturday was beginning for everyone else.

And set to work with an expression of grave, furrowed, master-craftsman concentration — squinting between his subjects and the paper, holding the charcoal up at arm's length to measure proportions he was not measuring, making small thoughtful *hm* noises, applying what looked from the outside like careful, deliberate cross-hatching.

The drawing was atrocious. Imogen came out as a potato with hair. Athena was a smaller, angrier potato with two triangles for ears and one enormous accusatory eye. The castle in the background leaned at an angle no castle should survive. The cross-hatching was four parallel lines applied with total false confidence.

He did not crack. Twenty minutes, dead serious throughout. Tongue between his teeth at the difficult bits.

At the end he signed the corner with a flourish — *10 Sept 1977 — ♥ CLW* — turned it round, and presented it with the solemnity of a Royal Academician unveiling a commission.

Imogen looked at it.

Athena, on her shoulder, looked at it.

Imogen's face went through several stages and arrived, helplessly, at tears of laughter.

"That's *me?* I'm a — Caleb, I'm a *root vegetable* — and you signed it like it's going in the *Tate—*"

They came in through the big oak doors. Saturday morning quiet — half the school still in dorms, the Hall in soft chatter not its weekday roar.

[[is there a Manet reference? because I was talking about titanic]]

## Narrator

OOC: Caught. The *one of the French girls* phrasing has been double-tracked in our records — the 7 Sept retcon commit reframed it as a *classical odalisque / French academic studio life-drawing* art-history reference (i.e., Manet's *Olympia* and that tradition), which is how Imogen, as the artist, would read it in 1977. That was the in-fiction frame.

Your OOC frame is the actual one: the *Titanic* (1997) "draw me like one of your French girls" line — Kate-Winslet-says-it-to-Leonardo. Caleb's playing a long-game knowing the line *will* land in 1999, because you the user know the film and Caleb (channeling you) is making a 22-year forward bet on a line he knows will eventually be famous. That's the actual joke — Mei calling it a Manet reference is in-fiction true; the meta-truth is Caleb is gambling on cultural-event-from-the-future.

Mei calling it a Manet reference *can stand* as her in-fiction read (she doesn't have access to 1997), but I shouldn't put it in your mouth or treat it as the joke's real anchor. Re-running Mei's last line cleanly:

Mei, dry, to Caleb:

"You have built a comedy investment portfolio. Time horizon twenty-two years. Underlying asset: *whatever you are seeing that the rest of us are not.* I am — proud and a little frightened."

Same beat, no reference-claim. Carry on from there.

Light-footed. The hand barely registered the weight.

Aisling did not jump. Did, however, draw breath sharply in.

## Narrator

▼ Saturday, 10 September 1977 — 09:20

He climbed through the portrait-hole behind Aisling — the small unfamiliar duck-and-step Gryffindors made dozens of times a day — and came into the warm round common room.

It was Gryffindor in a way the others weren't. Red velvet, gold tassels, a stag's head over the mantel with a knitted scarf draped around its antlers that almost certainly hadn't been there yesterday. A round wooden table in the centre, scarred and waxed by generations. Tall windows looking out east toward the lake. Two enormous armchairs flanking the fire — one occupied by a fifth-year crocheting and not looking up — and the long red sofa under the bulletin board.

Caleb's eye went straight to the board. The Hexfield notice was pinned at the top, Imogen's flag-art line-drawing in the corner. The sign-up sheet hung below it on a clipboard.

He crossed to it as Aisling's official Caleb-escort. Lifted the clipboard off its hook.

He glanced down the names.

About thirty. Aisling's at the top in her big confident hand — *AISLING DOYLE, 7th, will play.* Mairi second — *Mairi Gow, 7th, watching first match, may play depending.* Toby Greaves third — *will play, bringing chaos.* Then a column of names down the page in mixed hands — fifth-years, sixth-years, fourth-years dutifully signed *watching only* underneath Liri's wording. One name in particular caught his eye halfway down — *Marlene McKinnon, 6th, will play, has questions about the rules.*

Eight will-play, twenty-two watching. Plus three names with question marks.

▼ Saturday, 10 September 1977 — 09:21

The room paused.

A sixth-year at the round table set his quill down very carefully and asked, in the considered voice of a man who had heard several true things on top of each other:

"*MY ASS.*"

"The Ministry of Ye Ancient Sconce Safety. Junior representative. The auditing arm."

A fourth-year girl on the long sofa, with the proper Gryffindor *am I about to be a participant in something* face:

"...Is *that real.*"

Aisling, beside Caleb, kept a straight face with what was visibly significant effort.

"Cleared by McGonagall," she said firmly. "Sort of."

A different sixth-year — small, freckled, sharp-eyed, the kind of Gryffindor who collected information for sport — leaned forward.

"By her *not stopping us* on the way up, Niamh."

A long beat of properly considered silence.

She got up and went toward the dormitory stairs.

The fourth-year on the sofa, fully committed now:

Caleb gestured solemnly at her with the clipboard, granted authority.

"Granted. You're now the Junior Recording Officer of MY ASS. Find a quill. Begin when ready."

Aisling looked round. Athena, on her shoulder, also looked round — head turning a precise quarter at each wall.

Niamh nodded, set the ladder under the high one over the portrait-door, and went up the dormitory stairs to bang on Eamon's door.

The Junior Recording Officer (4th-year sofa girl, whose name turned out to be Brigid) was at Caleb's elbow with her own quill and a fresh sheet, properly poised.

Brigid wrote, fast and neat.

She crossed to look.

The room had now organised itself entirely around the audit, which was — Caleb noted as he wrote *MY ASS — Gryffindor Common-Room Survey, 10 Sept 1977 — Auditor: Waddell, C. — Recording: Brigid (4th, surname pending), Assisting: Doyle, A. (prefect)* at the top of his own sheet — exactly the speed at which Gryffindors did things.

The room had gone properly silent.

Niamh, slowly:

"...We have lived under flickering sconces for *seven years* and nobody has tried to *bop* them."

The fireplace did something.

The fire — which had been the normal Gryffindor red-and-gold — banked itself low in one smooth motion and re-lit with flames in *all four house colours simultaneously,* coiling round each other in a small contained spiral. It held the shape for about five seconds. Then collapsed back to ordinary fire.

The room — properly, this time — sucked in a collective breath.

Brigid wrote, hand shaking slightly: *1C + 2C simultaneous CW 15° = fireplace shows all four house colours for ~5 seconds. UNPRECEDENTED.*

Niamh, very slowly:

Athena, on Aisling's shoulder, ruffled all her feathers once — the proper *something interesting just happened in the room* registration — and resettled.

Caleb: "Maybe it has to be done daily and nobody's been doing it. *Sorcha — your job tomorrow morning. Bop sconce eight, fluff the cushions, log it.*"

The portrait nodded gravely and closed behind them as they stepped back into the corridor.

The Fat Lady was quiet for a long beat. The kind of long-beat that meant she was actually consulting her own memory, which portraits did differently than living people — slower, with the heavy turning-of-pages register.

"...Once."

Quietly.

"Nineteen-thirty-six. Two girls. Seventh-year. They came up to the corridor at a strange hour — perhaps two in the morning, perhaps three. They worked together at the wall for several minutes. I could not see what they were doing precisely — my frame faces inward — but I heard the hum. I heard the wall *open.* They went in. They came out about an hour later, holding hands. They never spoke of it to me, and I did not ask. They graduated in the summer of that year. I have not seen the seam since."

A small pause.

"*Their names were Imelda Fitzgerald and Beatrice Llewellyn. They are both, I believe, alive — last I heard, in nineteen-sixty-eight, they were still in Edinburgh together. Make of that what you will.*"

Aisling, very still:

"Madam. That is — *thank you.* That is — *useful.* And — *thank you for not telling me until I asked.*"

The Fat Lady inclined her head.

"*I tell what is mine to tell to those who ask the right question. Carry on, Doyle.*"

14C he took. Same protocol. *Nothing.* No response to any motion. Wrote it: *14C — non-responsive, possibly decayed or never charmed.*

15C, the last one, halfway between the prefect-bathroom door and the dead end at the far wall. Aisling at it. Push — nothing. Pull — *the candle flame turned bright orange and stayed.* She let go. The flame stayed orange.

He wrote it. *15C — pull = candle flame goes orange (stable), CW 30° = restore. Toggle, function unknown.*

"Brilliant. Hold them properly. I need *phials.* Glass, stoppered, three or four if anyone in this room has them. Or vials. Or anything that ends in -ial and holds a small amount of liquid clean. I need to take samples from a tap in the prefect bathroom. *Anyone?*"

Brigid was already turning to look across the common room.

"Sorcha — *don't you brew?*"

Sorcha brightened: "Aye. Got a small kit in my trunk. Phials and a stopper-set. Hang on—" She was already heading for the female dormitory stairs.

Caleb, to Brigid:

"Also, Mei and Imogen should be on the way up. The Fat Lady's expecting them. While we wait for Sorcha and them — *come through to the prefect corridor and the bathroom with me. You and Aisling and I have found things you should see properly with your own eyes before we go anywhere with this.*"

▼ Saturday, 10 September 1977 — 09:58

Mei took in the room — pool, taps, mermaid, dark oval — in one calm sweep.

"Right."

She did not say more yet. The cataloguing register on her face.

Caleb knelt at the pool rim. Uncorked one of Sorcha's phials. Carefully — with the small precise motion of a clock-repair son who knew how to handle delicate things — lowered the phial mouth into the dark oval at the surface. The oval did not disperse around the glass; if anything it *flowed inward,* into the phial, dark fluid filling the small space cleanly until the oval was gone from the pool surface and the cold water beneath was, again, clean cold water.

He capped the phial. The dark liquid inside it stayed dark and held its shape — did not slosh, did not behave like water. Behaved like — a small captive amount of *night.*

Held it up.

"Got it."

Merphila, from her glass panel, made the small considered *correct procedure, child* expression.

▼ Saturday, 10 September 1977 — 09:59

Merphila's face came attentive — the *good question* attentive, not the *correct procedure* one.

"*The -philos lands. Thank you, child. My given name is not for me to tell you yet — I will know when, and so will you. The placeholder will do for now.*"

Sorcha took the phial with both hands, eyes round.

He turned to the spiral tap. Mei watched. Aisling watched. Brigid wrote.

He turned the spiral.

What came out was — *moving.* The fluid that emerged from the spout was *clear,* like spring water, but it *moved with intent* — the falling ribbon coiled in a slow spiral pattern as it fell, on its own, against the gravity that should have pulled it straight. Hit the pool surface and *kept spiralling,* a small clear vortex perhaps four inches across, embedded in the otherwise still cold water, turning slowly anti-clockwise.

It did not mix. It held its spiral.

Merphila's face came properly attentive.

"*Spiral is path-water. The pool was once filled with it for finding-ritual. Of the three, the most particular about intent. I would not stand directly above the small vortex without holding a question you genuinely want answered.*"

*see if my wand interacts with it. see if I can pull it up into a vial. fill a vial with dew and one with path-water"

## Narrator

▼ Saturday, 10 September 1977 — 10:00

He took his wand out — cherry, eleven-and-a-quarter, springy — and lowered the tip toward the small embedded vortex on the pool surface. Not touching the water yet, just letting the tip hover a finger's-breadth above the spiralling clear column.

The vortex *registered* him. The slow anti-clockwise turn changed pace for half a second — a small acknowledgement-ripple radiating out from where his wand-tip pointed. Then resumed normal speed.

His wand-tip did not glow. Did not warm. Did not vibrate. But there was a faint *pull* through the wood — the same pull he felt in dousing-rod work back in Manchester, when his father had let him try once with a hazel switch over a buried pipe. A *yes-there-is-something-here* pull. Quiet but present.

He withdrew the wand.

He took a fresh phial. Lowered the open mouth above the spiralling vortex. Carefully — not into it, just above it.

The path-water *rose into the phial of its own accord,* against gravity, in a thin clear spiral ribbon that filled the phial in three seconds and stopped at the cork-line. He capped it. The fluid inside the phial continued to *spiral very slowly,* clockwise this time, perhaps because the phial had flipped its orientation. *It did not stop.*

He set it aside on the pool rim. Took the third phial. Crossed to the star tap, turned it briefly, filled the third phial with *dew,* turned it off. Capped.

Three phials. Dew. Night-water in Sorcha's keeping. Path-water in his pocket now.

Mei, watching, very quietly:

"Caleb. *These are not the kind of phials you put on a Slughorn supply shelf. These are three of the oldest substantives I have ever seen in one place. Sorcha — your grandmother is going to want to come to the castle.*"

Sorcha, both hands now around her night-water phial:

"*Aye. She is.*"

Caleb — this is the most magic I have seen at once in my entire life. I am drawing it all and I am also slightly afraid.*"

## Narrator

▼ Saturday, 10 September 1977 — 10:02

Merphila's face came attentive again — this time the *now you are asking the actual question* attentive.

"*Yes.*"

A breath of underwater half-voice.

"*He was a Welshman of the same village as my model — Bala, on the shore of Llyn Tegid. His name in this castle's records is rendered as Hywel Bevan, master-glazier, contracted 1671 for refurbishment work on the prefect facilities. His name in his own tongue was Hywel ap Ifan ap Owen Bevan, of the line of the Bevans of Llanuwchllyn. He was a witch's son, the youngest of nine, the only one with magic in him. His mother was Annwyl Bevan, who was a hedgewitch of the old Cymric tradition — the keepers of dew and night and path. The substantives he installed here are her three.*"

She paused.

"*She came up to the castle once with him to bless the installation. The records do not show it because she had no formal magical credentials and Madam Pince's predecessor did not log such visits. But she was here. The shrine — yes, child — is hers, not his. He built the housing. She filled it. The mermaid is the gatekeeper. The name — when it comes — will be hers, not mine.*"

Another small considered pause.

"*She has been dead since 1689. The shrine is intact because her son installed it carefully and because no living wizard since has put the right questions to the glass.*"

## User

"are you her?"

## Narrator

▼ Saturday, 10 September 1977 — 10:03

Merphila held entirely still.

A long underwater pause. The kind of pause a glass figure could hold without strain because she did not breathe.

Then her face softened — the proper *you have asked the only question that matters* softening.

"*Some of her. Not all of her. A piece she put into the glass when she sealed the shrine, so the gatekeeper would not be a stranger to the petitioners. She put a year of her work into me — the dew was her milk, the path was her walking, the night was her listening. She was forty-seven when she did the work, recently widowed, her son had asked her up to the castle because he was frightened of installing his mother's substantives wrong and wanted her hand on the work. She came. She stayed for the spring of 1672. She put what she could spare of herself into me — the gatekeeping part, which is the listening-and-judging part, which is the part of her that had been a hedgewitch for thirty years.*"

A small breath.

"*She went home in May. She lived another seventeen years. She did not come back. The full of her is buried in Llanuwchllyn churchyard, which is consecrated ground; the rest of her is in the dew tap, the night tap, the path tap, and the glass that holds me. When her name is spoken to me by someone with the right key, all of those wake up at once, and the shrine becomes operable as she designed it.*"

She looked at Caleb properly.

"*You are the first to ask me. In three hundred and five years. Thank you, child.*"

▼ Saturday, 10 September 1977 — 10:04

Merphila was very still.

When she spoke again, her voice was — *quieter than it had been. Smaller. Closer to a hummed note than to speech.*

"*Child.*"

A pause.

"*Yes. The shrine is a son's grief made into a working magic, because he could not bear that his mother would not be seen by the wizards he had been told would be its custodians. He installed her here in the place where the children of the magical world would bathe and grow and pass their daily lives, and he asked her to wait, and she agreed. He believed — and she agreed — that someone would, eventually, ask the right question. They were both prepared to wait centuries.*"

A second small pause.

"*You have asked. That is not nothing. That is the thing they were waiting for, in the way a thing is sometimes waited for — not for what comes after, but for the asking itself. The asking is the offering. The opening is — secondary.*"

Her face did something that might, if she had been a person, have been the small wet smile of someone moved.

"*I do not know whether you will find Annwyl's name. The name is the harder of the two keys. The pitch is mechanical and you will solve it with patience. The name is — *in archives,* if it is anywhere. Llanuwchllyn parish records. Bala village papers. The Welsh ministry magical census of 1660 which lists hedgewitches by their craft-name. Possibly Hywel's contract paperwork in this castle's own building archive. The path-water phial you gave Sorcha may, if she is willing, help her grandmother find it in less time than it would take any of you. The path-water is a finding-substantive. It is for this.*"

A beat.

"*But you have asked the question, child. The shrine is already partly opened by that.*"

## Narrator

▼ Saturday, 10 September 1977 — 10:06

They filed out of the bathroom in the order they had come in — Caleb last, the door closing soft behind him on Merphila in her glass panel, the half-filled pool, the empty stillness of a shrine that had agreed to wait a little longer.

The prefect corridor felt — *different now,* though nothing in it had changed. The sconces sat in their brass. The trick of the eye was that one of them now knew what the corridor was *for.*

Aisling led the way back to the portrait-hole. The Fat Lady watched the line of them pass back through.

"*Doyle. The wall has not collapsed. I take this as evidence.*"

"*Wall is intact, Ma'am. Thank you.*"

In the common room Brigid took her place at the central table and pulled the master sheet to her properly. The three returned audit-team reports sat in columns. Sorcha tucked the brewing pouch with its three precious phials into the inner pocket of her robes, double-checked the cork-fits, and sat down beside Brigid to add the bathroom findings to the master record.

Mei found a chair near the fire and pulled out a fresh roll of parchment from her satchel — she had brought it knowing she'd need it. Began to draft, in her clean precise hand, the executive synthesis: *Finding by Caleb Waddell, Aisling Doyle, and the Gryffindor common-room audit team, 10 September 1977. To: Professor McGonagall.*

Imogen, standing at the window, was sketching — the bathroom from memory, while it was still fresh, with quick certain strokes. Athena had moved to the back of Mei's chair and was watching the proceedings with the proper supervisor posture.

Caleb stood for a beat, just looking. The Gryffindor common room mid-morning Saturday — warm, red, working, the three new deputies fully invested, Mei drafting on a chair that was not hers, Imogen capturing a thing that had taken three centuries to be seen.

It was, he thought without quite finding the word for it, a *good* room to be in.

*I go into the library with them*

## Narrator

▼ Saturday, 10 September 1977 — 10:12

He pushed the heavy library door open. They came in together — Caleb leading, Aisling behind, Mei third, Imogen last with the sketchbook against her chest now in deference to the silence.

The Hogwarts library on a Saturday morning — high vaulted ceiling, long shelves running back into the dim, dust-motes in the slants of light from the high windows, the deep proper *library silence* that was more than just quiet. About a dozen students scattered at tables. A pair of Ravenclaw sixth-years at the long centre table doing essays. A Hufflepuff fourth-year asleep with her face on a book the way her counterpart in Gryffindor had been an hour earlier.

No Pince visible on the floor. The librarian's high desk by the door was empty.

Caleb felt Athena adjust her grip in his sleeve at the change of light. She did not stir further.

Aisling pointed west — the small reading room off the main floor, glass-panelled door, a single light burning inside. They crossed quietly.

Through the glass: Mairi Gow at the chair by the window. Dark plait at the nape. Book open in her lap, second book open on the small table beside her. Reading. The small *I am completely present in this text* posture Aisling had described.

She looked up at the four of them coming through the door before any of them had made noticeable noise.

Her eyes went — Aisling, Mei (registered: prefect of another house), Imogen (registered: Hufflepuff), Caleb. Then to Caleb's left sleeve, where the cuff sat slightly wrong-shaped at the elbow.

Did not say anything.

Marked her place in her book with one finger, closed it carefully, set it on her lap, and waited.

## Narrator

▼ Saturday, 10 September 1977 — 10:13

He left them at the reading-room door — Aisling already raising a hand at Mairi through the glass, Mei stepping in to greet her properly — and slipped backward into the main floor of the library.

Then started moving.

Tiptoed past the first set of shelves with exaggerated *I am being silent* high-knees, the way a cat-burglar in a Muggle comic-strip moves. Ducked behind the second row. Pressed back against the spines as if pursued. Peered out around the end of the shelf. Made eye contact with a sixth-year Ravenclaw at the centre table who was watching him with growing alarm. Tipped his chin at her in courteous *yes, this is happening* greeting.

Hopped to the next row. Crouched behind a low cart of returned books. Crab-walked four feet. Stood up suddenly between two shelves with both arms wide, as if announcing his arrival to nobody.

Athena, in his sleeve, registered the changes of orientation but did not stir — *if anything, settled deeper,* the small warm presence at his elbow taking the bumps as part of the ride.

The Hufflepuff fourth-year had now woken up and was watching him with both hands flat on her open book, not blinking.

Through the glass of the reading-room he could see Mairi — who had not moved from her chair — *watching him* through the glass with the small Mairi *I am observing this on the record* expression, while Aisling, beside her, had her face in her hand and was, evidently, trying not to laugh.

The Ravenclaw sixth-years had given up on essays.

## Narrator

▼ Saturday, 10 September 1977 — 10:14

He crouched at the end of a low aisle, opened his sleeve, and Athena stepped down carefully onto the polished oak floor. Looked up at him expectantly.

"Right. *Army crawl.* Watch and learn."

He went prone — *fully prone, on his elbows and toes,* in his school robes, in the middle of the Hogwarts library — and began advancing in the proper military low-crawl shape he'd seen exactly once in a Muggle war film at the Manchester Odeon in 1973.

Athena watched him from the floor. Head tilting one way, then the other.

Then she walked alongside him at her own unhurried owl-walk, matching his pace without effort. The two of them advanced toward the empty librarian's high desk by the front door — Caleb low and dignified and inching, Athena upright and small and entirely unhurried. She did not crawl. She did not understand that there was crawling on offer. She walked.

He glanced sideways at her, mid-crawl.

"*Like this. Like this.* Get *low.*"

Athena, who was already approximately four inches off the ground when standing, made the *I am as low as it gets, sir, what are you on about* head-tilt. Continued walking.

The Ravenclaw sixth-year at the centre table had a hand over her mouth. The Hufflepuff was filming this in her brain for the rest of her life. Through the reading-room glass, Mairi's expression had not changed — but Aisling had now turned her face into Mei's shoulder, and Mei was patting her back with the patient hand of a witness to events.

## Narrator

▼ Saturday, 10 September 1977 — 10:17

He stood up from the floor in front of the high desk, brushed off his robes, and lifted Athena onto the polished wood beside the inkwell.

Pulled a clean sheet of parchment from the small stack Pince kept for borrower-notes, took her quill from the brass stand, opened the inkwell. Athena watched the proceedings with bright professional interest.

He wrote, neat schoolroom hand:

*Dear Madam Pince,*

*I have broken your rule and brought Athena into your library outside her permitted visit. I apologise without reservation. The circumstances are: a finding has been made in the prefect bathroom of Gryffindor Tower that I will be presenting to Professor McGonagall in the next hour, concerning a 1672 installation by a Welsh master-glazier named Hywel Bevan — Hywel ap Ifan ap Owen Bevan, of the line of the Bevans of Llanuwchllyn — and a mermaid glass panel which has revealed itself as the placeholder-named gatekeeper of a small working shrine.*

*The shrine appears to have been built by Hywel for his mother — a Welsh hedgewitch of the old Cymric tradition, keeper of dew, night, and path — whose given name we do not know and whose recovery is one of two keys to opening the shrine properly. She was the youngest of nine grown children with magic in only her, recently widowed at 47 when her son asked her up to the castle to bless the installation, lived another 17 years and is buried in Llanuwchllyn churchyard.*

*I write to request your guidance: any reference you know of to Hywel Bevan's contract paperwork (1671), the Welsh ministry magical census of 1660 (said to list hedgewitches by their craft-names), Llanuwchllyn parish records, or the Hogwarts building archive for the prefect-bathroom refurbishment of 1671-72 would be of immense help in locating his mother's name.*

*I also write to ask, with the precedent of Hesba Ollivander's* Companion Owls — A Quiet History *(1922) before us, whether you would reconsider your standing rule on the visit-frequency of companion-bonded owls. I have, since the rule was set, found that being separated from Athena for long study sessions in your library exceeds my capacity for proper scholarly attention. I do not ask for an exception today — today's breach is mine, and I accept the consequences. I ask, instead, that you consider whether the bond-relationship described by Hesba might merit a different category of access for genuinely-bonded pairs going forward.*

*With apology and proper respect,*

*Caleb Waddell, Ravenclaw 7th-year*

He blew on the ink. Folded the letter once.

Then carefully dipped Athena's foot into a small puddle of ink — Athena registered the dip with the expression of *we are about to do a thing I have not approved* — and set her on the foot of the letter.

She hopped, two small considered owl-hops. Looked up at him for further instruction. Hopped again. Three more prints around the signature, the fourth slightly smudged. Two thoughtful prints up the right-hand margin.

He wiped her foot clean on a borrowed corner of his hem, scratched her crown in apology, tucked her back inside his sleeve.

Set the letter square on Pince's desk.

## Narrator

▼ Saturday, 10 September 1977 — 10:18

He took her back out of his sleeve, set her on the polished oak of Pince's high desk, and laid a fresh sheet of clean parchment in front of her. Tipped a small careful puddle of ink to one side of it.

He crouched to her eye level.

"I'm sorry I dipped your foot, sweetheart. That wasn't fair. But you're beautiful, and I would love a little reminder of you. Would you step in the ink with both feet, hop around the parchment for me a bit, and then I'll clean you up properly. For me. *Only if you want.*"

He held the want clean too — *for me, your prints, however you make them, completely your choice.*

Athena considered him. Considered the parchment. Considered the small ink puddle. Tilted her head.

Then — *deliberate decision* — she walked into the puddle, both feet, settled them properly, lifted one foot and set it down on the parchment, then the other. Took a small considered step. Another. Hopped sideways. Then forward. Then turned in a half-circle.

She was *making a small composition,* not a random scatter. The careful precision of a creature who had been asked for a gift and understood the request.

She finished. Looked up at him. Made the small *I have made you a thing, look at it* chuck-chuck sound.

Caleb looked at the parchment.

About fifteen owl-prints in a loose curving spiral — beginning at the centre, working outward in a small clockwise arc. The print at the centre slightly heavier than the others, as if she'd stood there a beat longer. The spiral ended at the edge of the parchment with one final small step that was *not* part of the spiral but a little aside from it — a *kiss-print,* almost. A signature off to the side.

He breathed out properly. Did not say anything for a moment.

Then, quietly:

"*Athena. That is the most beautiful thing anyone has ever made me. Thank you.*"

He picked her up carefully — feet still inky — set her on the corner of the desk, and used the borrowed corner of his hem to clean her properly: both feet, between each claw, the small ink-residue at the back of her tarsus. She let him do all of it, eyes half-shut, the small *thank you for the cleanup* posture.

He scratched her crown when he was done. Folded the parchment with the spiral very carefully, ink-side in, and slid it into the inside pocket of his robes where the proposal had lived earlier.

He stepped up to the stone gargoyle at the foot of the staircase — squat thing, granite, frog-jawed and judgemental in the way Hogwarts statuary tended to be — and, before any of the other four could intervene, leaned in and kissed it firmly on its stone lips.

A small dignified beat.

The gargoyle's eyes opened. Granite eyelids creaking. The frog-jaw worked once, twice. Then it spoke, in the dust-and-mortar voice of a creature that had not been kissed in approximately several centuries.

"*Well.*"

A long Scottish *well.* The voice of a stonemason in 1142 who had carved a face onto a guard-statue and not expected affection from it in his lifetime or any successor's.

Then she removed her spectacles. Cleaned them slowly on the cuff of her tartan-collared robe. Replaced them.

Aisling, in Caleb's sightline, did the smallest *I called this exactly* shift of her shoulders.

"Mister Waddell."

The dry careful tone.

"You arrived at this castle in September of 1971. In the six years since, you have been a perfectly competent Ravenclaw student, with the occasional notable mark on a Charms practical and the recent — *significant* — coursework on the Brigham office, of which Professor Flitwick has spoken to me at appropriate length."

A pause.

"I have not, in six years, had cause to summon you to this office. This morning at breakfast I observed you working a recognised Manchester clock-repair shoulder-pressure technique on Miss Doyle in the Great Hall, I observed your owl — Athena, I believe — enter the Great Hall on foot through the main doors and walk the length of the central aisle to your bench, I observed you and Miss Doyle leave together with the owl on her shoulder, and I have now received you, ninety minutes later, in my office with two of the four other seventh-year prefects, a Hufflepuff candidate for the magical-creatures-Healer track, and a folded letter on my desk which I will read in a moment."

She took a small careful sip of her tea.

"The pace of your morning, Mister Waddell, is — *not unprecedented in this school's history, but rare.* I would like to know what is happening to you. I will get to the shrine and the room in a moment. First — *are you well?*"

Merphila has stated openly that the shrine requires two keys to open properly: a *pitch* — which she has not specified beyond suggesting it travels through the right water — and a *name* — which is the mother's given name, held in archives we cannot access without staff assistance. ▼ Saturday, 10 September 1977 — 10:30

Aisling resumed. The room had settled into the proper briefing rhythm now — McGonagall sipping her tea steadily, the four of them in semi-formal arrangement, Caleb in the junior chair listening.

"The hidden room finding is separate but parallel. Sconces eleven and twelve in the prefect corridor — *push, pull, single twist each direction, bop* did not produce anything alarming individually. Eleven on a counter-clockwise twist of thirty degrees emits a sustained tuning-fork hum. Twelve on a clockwise twist of ninety degrees lengthens the corridor by approximately three feet. Simultaneous opposite twists held for two seconds produced a vertical seam between them — a forming-door — about six inches wide, with the eleven-hum deepening to the resonance of an enclosed space of substantial size. We released within two seconds. The seam closed cleanly. The corridor returned to its standard length."

A breath.

"We did not enter. We did not test further."

She paused. Glanced toward the Fat Lady's testimony Mei had transcribed onto the second page of the executive summary.

"The Fat Lady has volunteered, on direct question, that she has seen the seam open *once* before — in nineteen thirty-six, by two seventh-year Gryffindors, Imelda Fitzgerald and Beatrice Llewellyn, who came up to the corridor between two and three in the morning, worked at the wall for several minutes, the wall opened, they went in and came out approximately an hour later holding hands. They did not speak of it to her, and she did not ask. They graduated that summer. They are, by the Fat Lady's last knowledge in nineteen sixty-eight, both alive and in Edinburgh."

## Narrator
