## Narrator

Continuing the search.

Compiled. Printing here as a reference record I can refer back to throughout the McGonagall conversation:

**PATH REFERENCE RECORD — assembled from transcript, locked for Caleb's McGonagall explanation**

**The book.** *Liber Herbarum Privatus Magistri Cuthberti de Lindesia, Anno Domini MCCCXIV* — *Private Book of Herbs of Master Cuthbert of Lindsey, AD 1314.* Bought from Caractacus Burke at Borgin and Burkes, Wed 31 Aug 1977 ~20:31, for 3 Galleons. Burke set conditions for the third object (not this one): time + repeat custom + a referrer + a real problem.

**The locks.** Front-cover lock = three concentric brass rings + medieval Latin riddle *TRES SUM. PRIMVS HOMINI VITAM DAT. SECVNDVS DOLOREM TOLLIT. TERTIVS MORTEM DIFFERT. QVI ME APERIT, OMNES TRES NOMINAT.* Answer: **VIS** (vital force). Cuthbert's epigraph: *if you read these words, you have understood vis. Take care that you understand the rest as well.* Spine lock (hidden, second) released after front rings set to **NEX** (violent death). Hidden quire of four pages came free from the back binding.

**The 17 words, named in order.** **VIS — UMBRA — NOMEN — SANGUIS — CINIS — VOX — SIGILLUM — SOMNIUM — CARO — NUMERUS — NIHIL — STELLA — AURUM — OS — LIMEN — SILENTIUM — FINIS.** (Force, shadow, name, blood, ash, voice, seal, dream, flesh, number, nothing, star, gold, bone, threshold, silence, end.)

**The settings.** Each of the 17 interleaved pages opens with a verb of perception (*vidi, audivi, sensi, inveni*) in one of nine settings: **nocte, aqua, silva, dormiendum, speculo, terra, flammis, mortuos, sidera.** (Night, water, forest, sleep, mirror, earth, flames, the dead, stars.)

**The patient.** **AEDITH WULFRICSDOHTER** — 11 years old, parish of Cuthbert, 1311. Walked the Path involuntarily through 17 visions across one season in the 9 settings. Named all 17 in sequence. At the 17th word (*silentium*) she went silent and *remained alive in some quiet condition* — Cuthbert's phrase. Did not die. Did not heal. Cuthbert distinguishes between her *silentium* (failure-mode) and a properly walked one (destination). Her seventeenth was not the right kind of silentium.

**Cuthbert.** Witness, not walker. Recorded the 17 in order. Worked out retrospectively that what Aedith did involuntarily and incompletely, a person could do deliberately and correctly. Refused four petitioners who came to him asking for the Path. Named them so the reader would know *others have wanted this.*

**The four petitioners** (Cuthbert's list, hidden quire page 2):
1. **Rudibartus de Stainford** — minor wizard, West Riding. Walked partially ~1320. Lived another 20 years quietly, no further notable practice.
2. **Richard of Kirkham** — Cumbrian. Walked it differently ~1325, reached undocumented end-state, vanished from parish 1328, Council marked him *missing rather than deceased* 1331.
3. **Margery the Little of Lacock** — Wiltshire wisewoman. Walked it to conclusion ~1330. Profound change. Lived in apparent good health and continued practice until 1372, died of unrelated old-age illness. **Her commonplace book** — held in the Lacock parish chest, *presumed lost during the Reformation,* possibly at the Cotswold Branch of the Magical Records Office at Cirencester. Almost certainly destroyed; *almost certainly* meaning *we have not actually checked.*
4. **Iohannes Capellanus de Sleaforde** — John the Chaplain of Sleaford. Listed by Cuthbert. **NOT in the Compendium** *Peregrini Infamiae* appendix. The gap itself is a thread.

**Cuthbert's warning.** *I do not know what the path is. I know what the path was: a young woman's seventeen seeings, in nine places, of seventeen named things, in this order. I know that some who learn of such paths seek to walk them. I do not advise it. The body is a herbalist's province; the soul is no one's. If you read this and you are tempted, lock the book again, and bury it. — Cuthbertus de Lindesia, scriptum manu propria, in nocte vigiliae sancti Iohannis, anno Domini MCCCXIV.*

**Caleb's read of the destination** (Option A blend locked in pre-compaction): walking the Path correctly grants (1) **interior magic** — wandless control of one's own body: hold breath for hours, slow heartbeat, resist Legilimency natively, resist Imperius, heal small wounds by attention, choose not to be afraid; selfish, only on self; emotional contagion dulls so the walker has to perform warmth previously felt automatically. PLUS (2) **a perceptual channel** — where memory clings to objects, who has lied recently, what a place was used for, where attention is really directed; narrow, fragmentary, but real; the channel does not turn off, the walker becomes harder for friends to read. Silentium properly walked = step out of magical detection (untrackable, un-Apparate-to, Dementor-and-magical-predator-undetectable) + ability to make a small bubble of quiet around oneself.

**The Compendium.** *Compendium Magistrorum Medii Aevi,* 3 volumes, kept at Brownson & Son, Diagon Alley. Volume I has Cuthbert (fl. 1295–1320, Lincolnshire herbalist, York Chapter House rolls 1297 & 1304). Volume III Appendix C *Peregrini Infamiae* originally had the Path roll. **The Path of the Seventeen was removed from public circulation in 1934 by joint recommendation of Magical Education and the Wizengamot.** Original held in restricted archive at the Department of Mysteries, accessible only by joint research warrant. Brownson's 1934 disclosure: the editorial committee was split on whether to suppress or publish. They suppressed because they could not agree whether Cuthbert's account was real or fabricated. They are still arguing about it.

**The Brownsons.** **Mrs Brownson** (thin, ~50, working robes) and **apprentice with keys** (name TBC). **Bartleby** the tabby cat (immovable, accepted Caleb via cheek-rub-on-fist). Mrs Brownson recognised the book on sight — tried to buy it from Burke in 1973, was refused. Burke kept it until someone asked the right question. Mrs Brownson asked Caleb to keep it safe. Standing welcome. Plan: first Hogsmeade weekend ~22 Oct, Floo from Three Broomsticks to Diagon, visit Brownsons, ask reader's-pass procedure for Cotswold Branch Magical Records Cirencester.

**The two recipes from the OPEN herbal** (not the hidden quire — Mei found these in the publicly-readable portion the Friday before term):

1. **AQUA DE TRIBUS SOPORIBUS — Three-Sleeps Water.** Poppy, valerian, small quantity of asphodel root, steeped under moonlight for 9 nights. Cuthbert's note: produces *a sleep of the body, a sleep of the mind, and a sleep of the third part* (Mei's read: the soul). No modern equivalent. *We do not make things in three any more.*

2. **UNGUENTUM MEMORIAE — Salve of Memory.** Beeswax, lavender, dittany, a single hair from the patient's own head. Applied to the temples while reciting the patient's full name. Cuthbert distinguishes carefully: for memory *taken,* not *lost* — counter-Obliviate. No modern equivalent known.

**The asphodel-page margin mark.** Three parallel strokes inside a circle. Matches *tres sum* from the lock. Only on the first 30 leaves of the herbal — Cuthbert's own *take note* mark.

**Recipes sent to Cassia Burbage.** Mei copied both recipes Sat 3 Sept evening from her Potions notebook; Caleb did the fair-copy in Latin same evening at the Ravenclaw west-window table. **Sheet headed:** *From a fourteenth-century English herbal, anonymously transcribed for your assessment.* Sent Monday 5 Sept via common-room post (Caleb's covering letter went with the parson letter; recipe sheet went separately). Cassia's expected reply window: 5-7 days from posting = **8-12 September** (today 10 Sept is inside the window, reply could land any morning).

**Cassia's standing instruction (Thu 1 Sept ~07:47, Slug & Jiggers back workroom):** transcribed herbal pages OK, 2-3 at a time, by letter, autumn-winter; NO copies of the hidden quire by post, in her flat, or in the shop. The recipes are within this window because they come from the open herbal, not the hidden quire.

**The parson letter.** Mei wrote it. Asks the current vicar of (parish TBC) for any record or recollection of *the south-wall area, of Aedith Wulfricsdohter, or of the pre-Reformation parish chest.* Sent Monday 5 Sept same post as Cassia. Reply window 7-14 days; both hopefully by 20 Sept.

**Working hypothesis (Lin's, Sun 4 Sept):** The visions and the Path may be *separate phenomena.* Cuthbert may have reverse-engineered the Path from Aedith's visions retrospectively. The visions may have been imposed by something external; the Path may be a structure Cuthbert assembled from her sequence. *He is the one who decided that her seventeen visions described a Path. He says so himself — I have set the path down here as she gave it to me.* Open question.

**Open questions Caleb carries:**
- Did Cuthbert walk the Path? (Currently uncertain. Possible: not at all / partially / wholly.)
- Who was Iohannes Capellanus de Sleaforde and why is he the missing fourth?
- Does Margery the Little of Lacock's commonplace book actually survive at Cirencester?
- What is the destination Cuthbert hinted at (the proper *silentium* that is *being able to go quiet,* not *going mute*)?
- What relationship, if any, between the Path and the Hexfield-prefect-bathroom shrine threads. (None obvious. But two old magical things, both involving silence/listening/walking/dew-and-night-and-path — Annwyl's substantives = dew, night, **path** — *that overlap is the question.*)

Now Caleb explains to McGonagall, with the above to draw from.

The Path storyline — the spine of Caleb's interior thread

The book: locked 14th-c. herbal by Magister Cuthbertus de Lindesia (AD 1314), bought from Caractacus Burke at Borgin & Burkes for 3 Galleons, 31 Aug. Two locks: front (VIS — solved) and spine (NEX — solved). Public contents: ordinary herbal, alphabetised. Hidden contents: a four-page quire on a single case from Cuthbert's parish in 1311.
Aedith Wulfricsdohter: an 11-year-old girl of Cuthbert's parish who experienced 17 involuntary visions across one season, in 9 specific settings (nocte, aqua, silva, dormiendum, speculo, terra, flammis, mortuos, sidera), naming 17 things in sequence: VIS, UMBRA, NOMEN, SANGUIS, CINIS, VOX, SIGILLUM, SOMNIUM, CARO, NUMERUS, NIHIL, STELLA, AURUM, OS, LIMEN, SILENTIUM, FINIS. After naming SILENTIUM (16th) she went silent. Did not die, did not heal, remained.
Cuthbert as witness, not walker. Refused four petitioners who came to him to learn the Path. Three of the four are recorded in the Peregrini Infamiae appendix of the Compendium: Stainford (walked partially, lived 20 more years quietly), Kirkham (walked it differently, vanished from his parish 1328), Margery the Little of Lacock (walked to conclusion, profound change, died 1372 of unrelated old age — her commonplace book is the only known second primary source). The fourth, Iohannes Capellanus de Sleaforde, appears nowhere in the Compendium — the gap is itself a thread.
Working hypothesis (Mei & Caleb, 1 Sept): visions and Path may be separate phenomena. Cuthbert may have reverse-engineered the Path out of Aedith's visions. Three readings of her silentium: end-state of walking, end-state of being walked-upon, or her own actions going wrong.
Two preparations in the herbal no longer in modern use: Aqua de Tribus Soporibus (Three-Sleeps Water — body/mind/soul) and Unguentum Memoriae (counter-Obliviate using beeswax, lavender, dittany, patient's own hair on temples while reciting full name). Cuthbert's marginal tres sum lock-mark appears on the Asphodel page only in the first 30 leaves.

Brownson & Son booksellers, four doors from Slug & Jiggers in Diagon Alley. She recognised the book on sight, tried to buy it off Burke in 1973. Original is in the Department of Mysteries restricted archive. Asked Caleb to keep the book safe. Tabby cat Bartleby. An apprentice with keys (unnamed). Standing welcome.

The 17 Ravenclaw common-room sconces — the master catalogue

Started by Hippolyta Vance (Ravenclaw, 1693 — distant ancestor of the modern Vance line; descent through Hippolyta unmapped but unbroken). Note: the catalogue is a Vance-family-line artefact — Cass's ancestor began it. Cass does not yet know this. Continued across three generations. Most recent entry 1881 noting the Brigham bust pair installation.

Hidden behind sconce #7 (west wall, between third and fourth windows). Twist #7 fifteen degrees clockwise → masonry above goes briefly transparent → reveals the catalogue parchment behind charm-glass. Hippolyta's own working journal is hidden behind sconce #5.

#	Location	Twist	Effect

2	North fireplace, right	90° clockwise	Returns single-word riddle answer through eagle door — bypass for one entrant

13	East wall, north of Rowena alcove	45° clockwise	Reveals small compartment with single rolled scroll — long-form Common Room By-laws of 1612 before they were inscribed publicly in the library

17	Small fireplace, east of eagle door	30° clockwise	Causes a small portrait of an unidentified Ravenclaw woman to materialise above the mantel for thirty seconds, who recites one couplet of verse from memory, then fades. Recorded couplets across centuries form a corpus

[[the skipping a generation has nothing to do with Mei being half]]

## Narrator

Caught — I conflated two separate facts. Mei is half-blood because **her specific parents** are one witch + one Muggle (Po-Lin + Wing-Hei). That's the whole reason, full stop. The fact that **Wing-Hei's first cousin Kai-Ming** is a wizard while Wing-Hei is a Muggle is a separate observation about magic appearing unevenly in the Chang patriline at the cousin level — it doesn't *cause* Mei's blood status. Mei being half-blood is determined at her own parent level, not by anything happening across the cousin line.

Fixing the doc:

Now fixing the Two-branch differences table where I'd implied the same conflation:

And the cohort doc had the same conflation:

Fixed. Now stated correctly as two separate facts:

1. **Mei is half-blood** because her specific parents are mixed — Po-Lin witch + Wing-Hei Muggle. Determined entirely at the parent level. *Full stop.*

2. **The Chang patriline carries magic unevenly at the cousin level** — Wing-Hei is a Muggle, his first cousin Kai-Ming is a wizard. This is a separate observation about how magic distributed across the patriarch's two sons' lines. It doesn't *cause* Mei's blood status.

The two facts are related (they both involve Wing-Hei being a Muggle) but neither causes the other. Mei could be half-blood with no patriline-skipping pattern at all (if Wing-Hei were the only Chang of his generation), and the Chang patriline could have magic-skipping without Mei being half-blood (if Wing-Hei had married a Muggle instead of Po-Lin, Mei would be a Squib or Muggle herself). Different mechanics.

## User

*continue with McGonagall*

## Narrator

▼ Saturday, 10 September 1977 — 10:33

He reached into his satchel and produced the small dark-leather book. Set it carefully on the corner of McGonagall's desk where she could see it without leaning.

"On Wednesday the 31st of August, in the evening, I went into Borgin and Burkes — Knockturn Alley — looking for something to do for an hour. Caractacus Burke was at his counter. I told him I was interested in things that *taught* rather than things that hurt — puzzles he might have that rewarded a careful reader. He showed me three. I bought this one for three Galleons."

He turned the book so the front cover faced her. The three concentric brass rings, the engraved medieval Latin.

"The lock has a riddle. *Tres sum. Primus homini vitam dat. Secundus dolorem tollit. Tertius mortem differt. Qui me aperit, omnes tres nominat.* The answer is **VIS** — vital force, three letters, the herbalist's word for the thing that gives life, takes pain, defers death. Once the rings are set to VIS the lock opens. There is a second lock in the spine, hidden, that opens to **NEX** — death."

A breath.

"The book inside is the private herbal of *Magister Cuthbertus de Lindesia, Anno Domini MCCCXIV* — Master Cuthbert of Lindsey, 1314."

McGonagall set her teacup down properly.

"...*Cuthbert of Lindsey.*"

She did not move for a long beat. Then she lifted the small book — careful, two hands, the way a museum-keeper would — and turned it in the lamp-light, looking at the rings.

"Mister Waddell. *That name is in a footnote of the* Compendium Magistrorum Medii Aevi *that I read as a postgraduate at the University of Edinburgh in nineteen fifty-eight.* I have not heard it aloud since. *Continue.*"

## User

.

## Narrator

▼ Saturday, 10 September 1977 — 10:33

"The front of the book is an ordinary herbal — fourteenth-century pharmacopoeia, alphabetised, routine plant medicine. Boring. Two-thirds of the volume." He paused. "Inside the herbal — every seventeen leaves apart — *seventeen interleaved pages.* Each begins with a verb of perception in Latin — *I saw, I heard, I felt, I found.* Each is set in one of nine places — *night, water, forest, sleep, mirror, earth, flames, the dead, stars.* Each is numbered *primus* through *septimusdecimus.* Each contains one larger word at its centre."

He took a small breath.

"The seventeen, in Aedith's order. *Vis. Umbra. Nomen. Sanguis. Cinis. Vox. Sigillum. Somnium. Caro. Numerus. Nihil. Stella. Aurum. Os. Limen. Silentium. Finis.*"

McGonagall did not write. She held the book. After he finished reciting she said, quietly:

"*Force. Shadow. Name. Blood. Ash. Voice. Seal. Dream. Flesh. Number. Nothing. Star. Gold. Bone. Threshold. Silence. End.*"

She had translated them from memory.

"*The Path of the Seventeen.* Mister Waddell. I am no longer hearing about a herbalist. *Continue.*"

## Narrator

▼ Saturday, 10 September 1977 — 10:33

"To get the spine to open I had to re-set the front rings to NEX and press where Burke had told me the second cipher would sit. The hidden quire came free from the back binding — four pages, folded, stitched in to lie flush. Cuthbert's hand throughout, but later ink — written some years after the herbal proper. He prefaces it: *I write this not as a herbalist but as a man who has lived past my craft. The herbs in the public book treat what the body does. These pages treat what the body cannot.*"

A breath.

"The quire is the case-record of a single patient. An eleven-year-old girl in Cuthbert's parish — **Aedith Wulfricsdohter**, daughter of Aelfgyth the smith's widow. Year 1311. Over one season she had seventeen visions in nine places and named the seventeen things in that exact order. At the sixteenth — *silentium* — she went silent. She did not die. She did not heal. Cuthbert's word for her condition was *remained.*"

McGonagall closed her eyes for one second. Opened them.

"*Silentium.* — The footnote in the Compendium said the girl *did not survive the seventeenth vision in any condition recognised by Cuthbert's contemporaries as life.* That sentence sat with me for thirty years."

A pause.

"She remained. Not died. *Continue, Mister Waddell.*"

## User

## Narrator

▼ Saturday, 10 September 1977 — 10:34

"Cuthbert is a witness, not a walker — that's his framing. He watched Aedith name the seventeen things over a season. Failed to save her. Wrote them down. *Afterwards* he worked out, retrospectively, what she had been doing — that the seventeen formed a *path*, that what she had done involuntarily and incompletely a person could in principle do deliberately. Her *silentium* he came to read as a failure-mode, not a destination."

A breath.

"He refused four petitioners who came to him asking to be taught. He named them so the future reader would know *others have wanted this*. *Rudibartus de Stainford. Richard of Kirkham. Margery the Little of Lacock. Iohannes Capellanus de Sleaforde.*"

McGonagall's hand stilled on the book.

"...*Margery of Lacock.* Mister Waddell. Tell me what Cuthbert said about her."

## Narrator

▼ Saturday, 10 September 1977 — 10:34

"Cuthbert's hidden quire says she came to him circa 1330 — twenty years after he wrote the case down. Lacock. Wisewoman. Asked him to teach her the Path. He refused her, like the others. He gives no further detail."

A beat.

"What the Compendium adds — *Peregrini Infamiae,* Volume III appendix — is that she *walked it to its conclusion* circa 1330, *underwent a profound change* recorded by her neighbours, and *lived in apparent good health and continued practice until 1372, when she died of unrelated old-age illness.* Her own commonplace book was deposited in the Lacock parish chest. Presumed lost during the Reformation."

McGonagall's eyes closed briefly.

"...*Margery walked it correctly.*"

She set the book down very carefully on the desk.

"Mister Waddell. I taught Charms briefly in the early sixties before Transfiguration became my proper assignment. Among the texts I worked through then was a small monograph called *On the Endurance of the Quiet Practice*, by a Welsh witch named Rhiannon Howell, published 1841. It is an obscure work. It survives in perhaps twelve copies in Britain. Howell's central claim — *that some witches and wizards have the capacity to be unobserved by the magical world, by deliberate practice rather than concealment* — was sourced, in her footnotes, to one earlier figure. **Margery of Lacock.** Howell cites a *Lacock commonplace book* that was, in 1841, *believed lost but possibly recoverable from Wiltshire ecclesiastical records.*"

A long pause.

"I have not thought about that footnote in fifteen years."

Athena considered McGonagall properly. Tilted her head a fraction. Made the small *chuck-chuck* sound that meant *I see, and I am being polite about it.*

McGonagall did not extend a finger. She did, very slightly, lean forward — the proper Scottish-formal posture of being introduced to a creature who deserved the same register as a small person.

"*Miss Waddell.* I am glad to meet you formally. I have, of course, watched you walk the length of my Great Hall on foot this morning, and I am writing it down in my own diary later. ## Narrator

▼ Saturday, 10 September 1977 — 10:38

He reached into his inner robe pocket and brought out the small folded composition — ink-side-in, careful not to smudge the kiss-print signature against the lining. Unfolded it on McGonagall's desk on the clear corner away from the herbal.

The clockwise spiral of fifteen owl-prints. The small smaller print at the spiral's centre — the *signature* one, properly intentional, the size of a thumbprint.

"In the library. On Madam Pince's desk. I was apologising and she was inkwell-side. Athena dipped her left foot and walked it clockwise from the outside in. Mairi was there. Mairi knew what it was."

McGonagall did not pick the paper up immediately. She bent forward over it with her spectacles on and looked at it properly — the spiral first, then the count of fifteen, then the placement of the signature print at the dead centre, then the trailing pressure-marks that told the order of the steps.

A long beat.

"Hesba Ollivander appendix two. *Given-marks.* The pattern is *clockwise spiral inward terminating in a smaller signature print of intent.* Two or three in a familiar-owl's lifetime, in the published cases."

She straightened.

"Miss Gow was right. *This is the real article.* Mister Waddell, you will keep this. You will not laminate it or do anything else to *fix* it — let the ink do what it does. You may, if you wish, copy the composition by hand on a separate sheet to share around. *The original stays with you.*"

Then — the small Scottish-formal pause that meant *now I am going to address the joke part properly.*

"And — on the *transfigured witch* hypothesis. *No, Mister Waddell.*"

She did not laugh. She did the very dry thing that was not a laugh but was something:

"I am — *as you may have heard* — a Transfiguration master and an Animagus. I would know within thirty seconds of being in a room with a transfigured witch. Your owl is an owl. She is, however, a *remarkable* owl. Hesba Ollivander would have written a chapter about her. *That* is the right register. — *Sentient companion making intentional marks within her own ethogram.* Not *human in disguise.* The distinction matters because the former is the proper Magizoology of a long bonded pair, and the latter is several extremely serious crimes."

A breath.

"Now. *Carry on, Mister Waddell.* Your Hexfield. Your fiancée. Your Saturday. Six o'clock for the book. — *Out.*"

The corner of her mouth had moved a millimetre when she said *out.*

## Narrator

▼ Saturday, 10 September 1977 — 10:39

Mairi slowed half a step as they came toward the main stair so she could explain it without the corridor echoing it back.

"The published cases are *given-marks found after the owl had died.* Someone goes through the owner's papers a week or a year after the funeral and finds a folded sheet pressed into the back of a book, with a clockwise spiral and a signature print, and only then does anyone realise what they're looking at. Hesba lists nine of them across two hundred years. None of the owners knew what they had been given while their birds were alive. The mark gets identified retrospectively — which is what *posthumous attribution* means."

A small breath.

"Yours was identified on the desk it was made on, in front of the bird who made it, by a witness who recognised the pattern within forty seconds. *That has not been documented before.* That is the first-of-its-kind thing McGonagall just confirmed."

Aisling, dry, alongside:

"And he is asking if his owl is a transfigured witch."

She did not say it unkindly. She said it the way Cork women say things to other women's husbands.

"Caleb. *The transfigured-witch idea is sweet and incorrect, and I love it, and I will tell Mei about it later and watch her face.* The interesting fact is the one Mairi just said. *You held still long enough for your bird to give you a mark, and a person who reads the literature happened to be standing there with you.* That is the story. Not the spirit theory. — *And yes, very good meeting.* You presented six unrelated mysteries before eleven on a Saturday and she liked your book title. *The book title.* Caleb, the book title was the funniest part. *Unauthorised corridor snogging* in a thesis abstract in McGonagall's handwriting. I have *peaked* before lunch."

The main stair opened ahead of them. The cohort traffic was light at this hour — late breakfast stragglers, a third-year on her way to the library with too many books.

Mairi, quietly, just to Caleb:

"On the spirit question — *not impossible, not what Hesba would call it.* Hesba's frame is that long-bonded familiars develop *toward* something that isn't quite what they were, by virtue of being read by a person for long enough. *Not human, not spirit, something the literature does not have a clean word for.* It is fine to sit with the question without naming it. The naming is what gets researchers in trouble."

▼ Saturday, 10 September 1977 — 10:39

Mei had been quiet for nearly twelve minutes in McGonagall's office and was the one who answered Caleb first, walking on his left.

"*Athena was sitting on the low branch of a hazel about a foot off the path when you saw her. — Eight in the evening, June, Macclesfield Forest. — You were alone, walking back to Cheetham Hill from your great-aunt's, on a path you had taken since you were eleven. — She was not in distress. She was not injured. She did not have a clutch she had fallen from — Pygmy Owls do not even live in Britain wild. — She let you walk up to her at arm's length. She stepped onto your wrist when you held it out. — You came home with her on your sleeve. — Caleb, the story is — she was waiting for you on a branch, on a path you walked, in a forest she should not have been in.*"

Aisling, sideways:

"*That is the version Mei has heard, and it is —* a story. *Caleb, you are not wrong that "she just is" does not answer it.*"

Imogen, beside Mei, quieter still — she had been the sketching-and-watching presence in McGonagall's office and had not spoken once in there:

"And she will not let me draw her in flight. She will let me draw her asleep, in the grass, on a perch, on Caleb's shoulder, on the desk. *Wing-spread in motion she turns her head from. Every single time.* I have noticed. I have not asked her why."

Mairi, on Caleb's other side, walking the way Borders Scots walk — even pace, low voice:

"Hesba's chapter four addresses the question and refuses it. *The owls find their wizards. The literature does not have a model for how. There are correlations — wizards in distress, wizards on the verge of bonding, wizards who have just lost a previous familiar — but no mechanism.* Hesba's framing is that the question is real but currently un-answerable by the tools available. *Not* that the answer is *nothing.*"

A small pause.

"The Pygmy Owl part is the part that should not be ignored. *They are not a British species. They are not migratory to here. They have been ringed in this country fewer than ten times in two centuries, and most of those were escaped collection birds.* Athena did not come from a nest in Macclesfield Forest. Where she came from is — *the actual unanswered question.*"

Aisling, after a beat:

"Caleb. *You're not wrong to be holding it. You are wrong about the transfigured-witch bit, but you are not wrong to be holding the question.* That is two different things and your fiancée's friend the case-study witness and the Hesba-quoting Border lass have all just said it back to you in their own registers. *Hold the question. Don't name it yet.*"

The main stair widened ahead.

Solomon woke briefly to nod him out, slept again behind the portrait. The corridor was empty. The main stair carried him down past the sixth floor and the fifth and the fourth — the castle in its Saturday late-morning lull, distant noises from the courtyard, a Hufflepuff third-year on the third-floor landing who blinked at Caleb passing and did not say anything.

The bond hum at his sternum stayed warm and settled and getting fainter with distance, which was its own kind of message: *still here, still working, fine.*

He took the east corridor off the entrance hall, found the still-life painting of the bowl of fruit between two suits of armour. The pear. He reached up and tickled it the way every Hufflepuff knew to.

The pear giggled. Turned into a green door-handle. The painting swung in.

The kitchens opened in front of him — vast vaulted ceiling, five long tables arranged exactly as the Great Hall above, copper everywhere, the smell of yeast and bacon-fat and something with star anise simmering — and a small house-elf at the nearest workbench looked up immediately.

"*Mister Waddell!*" she said, brightly, in the high reedy register house-elves used with students who came down on purpose. "*The young master is welcome! What is the young master needing?*"

## Narrator

▼ Saturday, 10 September 1977 — 11:08

He worked fast and quiet at the empty end of the table the first elf showed him to.

A round of soft yeast-dough she let him take from a tray that had been resting since the seven-o'clock prove. A jar of cream cheese already softened at room temperature. A tray of fruit left over from breakfast service — strawberries, raspberries, a melon, two kiwifruit she had to point him to, a tin of peach slices in light syrup, mint from the herb-shelf above the door, a small jar of honey, a half-lemon. The kitchens watched him out of the corners of their working without interrupting.

He rolled the dough into six small thin rounds, baked them off in the long oven at the second-to-end position the first elf set the timer on, and assembled the fruit pizzas while the rounds were still warm: cream cheese smoothed with honey and a scrape of lemon zest, mint chiffonade, fruit arranged outside-in in proper concentric rings, a final fine drizzle of honey through a stripped quill. Imogen-tart-spec discipline — *the size of her hand, the colour of late-afternoon sun* — applied to the fruit-ring colour balance.

While the last one cooled he set the table for **eight cups of tea.** Two heavy black-leaf teapots from the kitchens' staff shelf, eight small thick-rimmed cups of the kind the elves used between themselves rather than the polished house-cups for the Great Hall. Milk-jug. Sugar-bowl. A small saucer of lemon slices.

Sat down in the spot the first elf had nodded him to. Looked round properly at the elves who had drifted close: six of them, the first one across from him, the smaller pastry-elf to his left, a third on his right who had not spoken, two more at the end, and a sixth on the bench beside him with her feet not quite touching the floor.

"*Right.*" He picked up the nearest teapot. "*How do each of you take your tea?*"

A small ripple went round the table at that. The two at the end exchanged a look that Caleb could not read. The sixth elf, on the bench beside him with her feet not quite touching the floor, watched him very steadily and did not yet speak.

▼ Saturday, 10 September 1977 — 11:14

He kept it tight. Three minutes, no more — the kitchens were listening the way they had listened when he proposed coming to chop, but properly now, six pairs of small ears at the same table.

The Sconce Boy on the train from the great-aunt's, the lamps along the platform answering to him before he had learned what a charm even was. Mei at the welcoming feast last Thursday and the candles above the Ravenclaw table doing the small bow. Brigham's vellum and the seventeen-sconce catalogue in the common room, R5 holding Hippolyta Vance's journal at fourteen sustained for nine seconds, R3 and R4 a recognition-pair he and Mei had brushed three nights running. The Gryffindor audit this morning — 1C+2C the four-house unity flame, 3C+4C lifting the bulletin board off the wall, the 5C reading-halo. The prefect corridor, 11C+12C and the seam that opened to a hidden room behind. 13C+the prefect bathroom and a glass-panelled mermaid who answered to *Merphila* and pointed them at a sealed tile under the deep end of the pool.

He left the names of the petitioners and Aedith out. Saved them. The kitchens did not need the medieval-Latin file at the table.

It was the first word Wisp had spoken at the table.

Bramble drew a small breath. The kitchens at the surrounding tables had not slowed, but the six elves at this one were unmoving.

"Caleb. — *The kitchens have a sconce of their own.* — On the back wall, above the stockpot range, between the second and third arches. — *We do not touch it.* — *We do not point students to it.* — *The kitchens have known about it for at least Bramble's forty-six years and Marble's hundred-and-twelve, and the elves before us knew about it and the elves before them.* — *It has — done a thing — once, in Bramble's time. — *In nineteen fifty-three. — *And we have not spoken of it outside the kitchens since.*"

"*If Caleb wants to know what it did — *Caleb will need to ask properly. — *And Bramble will need Tippy's agreement to tell him. — *And Tippy will need to consider it.*"

Tippy held her cup with both small hands. She did not drink.

## Narrator

▼ Saturday, 10 September 1977 — 11:14

Athena, in the centre of the table, considered Caleb's intent — *would you like to go to her, if she would have you* — and turned her head toward Nessa.

Nessa, on the bench, did the small *yes please* hand-extension at lap-height — palm open, fingers loose, the proper way to invite a small creature without insisting.

Athena walked the five steps. Folded her wings tight, hopped from the table onto Nessa's open palm, walked the small distance to her wrist, and tucked there. Closed her eyes a quarter and waited.

Nessa lifted her free hand — small, careful — and used the very tip of her index finger to scritch the small soft place at the back of Athena's neck where the feathers fell into a parting. Athena's eyes closed the rest of the way. The small *chuck-chuck* came once. The kitchen elves at the table registered it without comment.

Bramble waited until the scritching had its rhythm. Then she spoke.

"Nineteen fifty-three. — A girl in her sixth year — *Rosamund Pierce* — Caleb. Half-blood. Came down to the kitchens at three in the morning in March. Bramble was on the night-prove with the bread. The girl had been crying in a corridor for some time before she found her way to the pear. We let her in.

"Her older brother had died the previous evening. Hit-wizards came to the school at first light to tell her. She had been in the Ravenclaw common room when it happened and had got herself out and down the staircases without anyone seeing her, and she ended up at the kitchen door because the kitchens were warm and bright. She sat at this table. Where Caleb is sitting now."

A pause. The kitchens worked quietly around them.

"The sconce above the stockpot range — *lit on its own.* Not bright. A warm small candle-glow. *And while it was lit, and while she sat at this table, no one could find her.* The Headmaster of the day — *Dippet* — had a locator going for her for the whole hour after the hit-wizards arrived. The locator never landed on her. We had her at the table. We fed her. The sconce stayed warm.

"She sat for two hours. When she was ready, she stood up. The sconce went dark. She left the kitchens, walked up to the seventh-floor Ravenclaw stair, and was found in her bed by the prefects ten minutes later — as if she had been there the whole time.

"She finished Hogwarts the next summer. She never spoke of the night. We never told. *We have never told,* Caleb. — *Until now.*"

Bramble considered for a beat. The kitchens know the Hogwarts roster going back decades — every student who comes through, every name on the doorstep at first-year. Pierce was a name she could check against her own memory without asking the others.

We notice these things. *A name that has been a Hogwarts name twice over — *parents at the school, sister at the school — *and then the second sibling does not appear — *is the kind of pattern the kitchens watch.*"

Marble, on Caleb's left, the small additional note:

## Narrator

▼ Saturday, 10 September 1977 — 11:15

Marble was the one who answered, properly. The 112-year-old pastry-elf who had known the family from before Rosamund was born.

"Henry Pierce, Ravenclaw, twenty-six to thirty-three. And Clara Beaumont, Gryffindor, twenty-seven to thirty-four. They met at the school. Henry was — *very quiet, very steady, prefect in his seventh year.* Clara was — *charming, very fond of singing, beat the bludger that broke her own collarbone at her last match and laughed about it for years.* Marble baked their wedding cake in nineteen thirty-six. Three tiers, blackcurrant, with a charmed glaze that held its shine for the whole reception."

A pause.

"Rosamund married in nineteen sixty-one, Caleb. Charles Ashwell — Slytherin a few years above her, the kind of Slytherin who *did not* go the wrong way. Quiet wizard, works in Wizarding archives at the British Library Magical Annex. They live in *Oxfordshire,* on the edge of the Cotswolds, not far from where Edmund — *where the family home was.* Two children, both magical, both still small. Rosamund is *Rosamund Ashwell* in the public record now. — Marble has a Christmas card from her every year. The Pierces — *Henry and Clara* — are still alive, eighty-six and seventy-nine, in the same Cotswold house. They did not leave. *They decided they were not going to be moved.*"

A breath. Marble looked at her hands.

"Marble has the Christmas-card address. *Marble will give it to Caleb if Caleb asks for it formally on his next visit.* Not today. Today is — *enough.*"

Tippy stood up off her bench.

"*The sconce, Caleb. — *Bring your tea.*"

She led him down the centre of the kitchens — past the long tables, the working elves at their stations who looked up and registered him passing and went back to their work — to the back wall.

The stockpot range was a long iron run of six pots simmering at low flame, the smell of stock and onion and bay. Above it, set in the stone wall between the second and third of the great vaulted arches that held up the ceiling, a small **sconce.** Plain iron. A single candle in the bracket. Unlit. The wax was old — twenty-four years of not being lit, properly old, the colour of weathered ivory.

Caleb stopped two feet short of it. Did not reach. Tippy, beside him, did not either.

▼ Saturday, 10 September 1977 — 11:31

He pulled a corner of one of Mei's draft schedule sheets toward him — the blank back of sheet six — and the room produced a pencil at his elbow without being asked. He tilted his head — *the pastry is the wrong shape* — pressed harder for the outer pastry crust, lifted the pencil — *the wrinkle goes here* — and the wrinkle came out as a row of little teeth.

## Narrator

▼ Saturday, 10 September 1977 — 11:31

He signed the toothy-crown drawing in the bottom right corner — *♡CLW 1977-09-10* in his proper neat hand — and slid it across to Imogen, who took it without looking, folded it once, and tucked it into the back of her sketchbook.

Then he stood up and went to the easel-and-table the room had set up by the right pane of the window for Imogen. The room had set out fresh supplies on the second tray it had produced — a small roll of cartridge paper at half-size, a stick of charcoal, a clean cloth, and a small lump of bread for erasing.

He took a sheet, set it on the slanted board at the side of Imogen's main easel, and picked up the charcoal.

It felt completely different from the pencil. The charcoal moved without the resistance the pencil had given him — it skidded across the paper at the first touch and laid a black streak much heavier than he had intended. He frowned, brushed at it with the cloth, lifted half of the streak, and tried again with less pressure.

Imogen, beside him, did not stop drawing her hippogriff but did turn her head a fraction.

"*Charcoal is — *not the pencil. — *Don't draw it, place it. — *Lift the stick off between each stroke. — *Think of it as printing the shape one piece at a time, not writing a line. — *And keep the cloth in your other hand — *charcoal is for fixing as you go, not after.*"

She demonstrated on the air over her own paper — a small *place-lift-place-lift* gesture, the cadence proper-Imogen-pace.

"*Try a circle.*"

## User

*try and listen*

## Narrator

▼ Saturday, 10 September 1977 — 11:32

He put the cloth in his left hand. Lifted the charcoal off the paper completely. Touched it to a clean spot at the top of the sheet — just touched, did not drag — and lifted again. A small black mark sat where he had put it.

He tried the second mark a quarter-inch to the right, the same way. Place, lift. Then a third. The three marks sat in a small arc the way three pebbles would on the rim of a glass.

Imogen, without turning her head:

"*Better. — *Now don't connect them. — *Add the fourth, fifth, sixth — *let the eye do the joining. — *The eye is faster at making a circle from dots than your hand is at making one from a line.*"

He added three more, each at the same slight curve-distance from the last. The six marks together looked, properly, like the start of a circle.

He closed his hand around the charcoal more loosely — Imogen's grip, he'd registered it from watching her — and added the seventh and eighth with less weight. The pressure changed and the marks went lighter, the curve continuing.

By the twelfth mark he had a circle of dots. He sat back. It looked — properly — like the rim of a custard tart seen from above. Not the tart. The *outline.*

"*Yes,*" Imogen said quietly. "*That. — *Now the surface. — *Charcoal flat on its side, very light, one pass across the inside of the circle. — *The colour-impression I said you got right last time, you can do that here properly because charcoal is *built* for tone. — *Pencil fights you on tone. — *Charcoal gives it to you.*"

He turned the stick on its side. Laid it across the paper inside the circle of dots. Pulled it gently from left to right in one slow pass. A pale grey filled the interior — too dark on the side he had started, lightening toward the end.

"*Almost,*" Imogen said. "*Lift evenly. — *Same pressure all the way across. — *The pull is — *all in the wrist, not the elbow. — *Try once more.*"

He brushed the inside with the cloth — half the grey lifted out — and tried again. This time the pass was even. Pale grey across the circle, the dots of the rim a little softer than they had been from the cloth-brushing.

The tart was on the paper. Approximately. In the proper colour-impression. Without teeth.

Mei, at the desk behind them, had stopped writing for the proper *I am watching the version of Caleb that learns* beat. Did not say anything. Picked the quill back up.

▼ Saturday, 10 September 1977 — 13:49

He walked to the announcer box, climbed the three steps up to the brass-cased radio, and gave it the kind of expectant pat a man gives a wireless when he wants it to know what mood he is asking for.

*Pre-event hype music. The kind that makes the back of your neck warm. Build-and-release rhythm. Drums. Clap-along beat. The thing that makes everyone in the hall feel like they have somewhere to be in two minutes.*

The radio considered, made the small *crackle-of-receiving* hum that wirelesses do when they are tuning, and *kicked in.*

The opening was a low pulsing drum on its own — a single deep thump every second, building. After eight thumps a second drum joined under it, faster. At sixteen thumps a clap-track came in on the off-beat, the kind of palm-clap that wanted the room to join. At twenty-four a synthesised brass line came over the top — properly 1977 in its texture, the way ABBA's *Gimme Gimme Gimme* opening felt before it had words, but built for a hall not a dance floor. The clap-track multiplied. By the thirtieth bar it was a *stadium-pre-match* wall of sound that wanted bodies to clap along with it whether or not the bodies had decided to yet.

Caleb's foot was already tapping.

Liri, at the announcer-box steps, made the small *I know what this is* Muggle-born laugh.

"*Caleb. — *Disco-floor instrumentation in a stadium-pre-match build. — *That is — *what arena announcers play before a Muggle football match in nineteen seventy-seven. — *But you've asked for the version that — *does not exist yet on the radio because it is too — *purpose-built. — *The Room invented it for you.*"

Mei, at the foot of the mounted bracket, stopped reading the schedule properly and turned. Her foot was tapping too. She did not seem to have noticed.

Caleb stood in the centre of the pitch with his wand at his throat, voice magically amplified and full of bright, teacherly enthusiasm as the two small teams took their positions.
“Alright everyone — eyes on the pitch! This is your live tutorial. We’re going to play and talk at the same time so you can see how it actually works. Gold on this end, Purple on that one. Flags are up!”
He gave the signal. The Room started the 5-minute clock, but this first demo round was deliberately loose.
“Rule one!” Caleb called as he jogged backward toward the Gold flag. “Bring the other team’s flag back to your own base! First team to do it wins the round!”
The whistle (blown by Liri from the announcer box) sounded.
Play started.
Caleb kept narrating as he moved:
“Legal spells only — Stinging Hex!” He fired a bright red jet at Marcus, who dodged laughing. “Rictusempra!” He caught Joanna with a tickling hex as she tried to flank. “Knockback! Trip Jinx!”
He even turned and fired a light Stinging Hex at his own teammate Lin, who yelped dramatically and dropped with a bright gold glow.
“See that?!” Caleb shouted happily, still running. “Even your own teammates can demonstrate! Lin’s down — glowing gold. He’s got thirty seconds!”
Imogen immediately slid in beside Lin, dropped to one knee, and placed both hands on his shoulder.
“Medic touch!” Caleb announced. “Fifteen seconds of contact and he’s back up instantly — watch!”
Imogen held firm. At fourteen seconds Lin’s glow vanished and he popped back to his feet with a cheer from the crowd.
“Beautiful work, Imogen! That’s the fast revive!”
Caleb sprinted toward the Purple end, still commentating between spells.
“Flag hold needs thirty uninterrupted seconds at the enemy base! No hitting the carrier while they’re holding it — but they’re very hittable on the way back!”
Mei darted past him like lightning, leopard-cat Patronus energy in every step. Cass nearly caught her with a trip jinx but Mei rolled beautifully and kept going.
“Mei’s on the flag run — classic sneaker behaviour!” Caleb laughed as he fired another knockback at Toby for good measure. “And yes — you can absolutely be dramatic about it!”
For the final two minutes they let the chaos breathe. Spells flew. Two more players went down and were medic-revived on the spot. Edward managed a brave but unsuccessful run at the Gold flag before getting tickled into submission. The crowd was laughing and pointing and leaning forward in their seats.
At the four-minute mark Caleb called it.
“Time! Demo round complete!” He lowered his wand from his throat, breathing hard and grinning ear-to-ear as both teams jogged back to the centre, flushed and laughing.
He addressed the crowd directly, still buzzing:
“So that’s Hexfield! Hits = glow for thirty seconds. Medic touch = instant revive. Run it back to base if no medic reaches you. Get the enemy flag, hold it thirty seconds at your base, and you score. Simple, chaotic, and stupidly fun.”
**PROPERLY THANK EVERYONE. people for coming and bringing a vision to life. my test players. Imogen as design of the field and sport and co-founder. Mei as my pair-bonded co founder. them for doing the rosters. the professors for coming to check it out. the elves for the food, which everyone is welcome to at any point. Liri for helping me announce and the show... anyone else***
“Questions? Comments? Anyone want to jump in for the proper matches? Liri? first match?”

## Narrator

▼ Saturday, 10 September 1977 — 14:06

Both teams jogged back to the centre flushed and laughing — Lin still rubbing his shoulder from the demo-fall, Edward wiping rictus-tears from his cheeks, Mei tucking her hair back into place with the proper *I rolled and it worked* face.

Caleb's wand at his throat again, voice carrying:

"*So that's Hexfield! Hits = glow for thirty seconds. Medic touch = instant revive. Run it back to base if no medic reaches you. Get the enemy flag, hold it thirty seconds at your base, and you score. Simple, chaotic, and stupidly fun.*"

The applause rolled — proper applause, not polite. The four-house mix of it audible by texture: Gryffindor whooping, Hufflepuff steady-clapping, Slytherin appreciative-tapping, Ravenclaw the *thoughtful regular beat.*

Caleb let it ride for a moment. Then turned the volume on his voice down a notch — the radio caught the shift and softened.

"*Before we go to proper matches, I need to do one thing.*"

The hall settled.

"*Thank you. — *All of you. — *For coming to a thing that exists because eight of us made it up in a Hufflepuff common room one Sunday afternoon and then we decided to invite the rest of you. — *Look around you. — *This room exists because you walked into it. — *That is — *the actual ask of every new thing, and you did it without making me beg. Thank you.*"

He turned toward the seven on the pitch with him.

"*My test players — *Imogen, Lin, Joanna, Edward, Cass, Marcus, Mei. — *You played this once when there were eight of us and a draft in a corner of the Room and you decided it was real. — *Without you there is no demo today. Thank you.*"

He pointed properly at Imogen.

"*Imogen Vale. — *Cofounder. — *Designed this pitch — *the geometry, the goals, the floor-plan. — *Designed the mascot banners that are currently moving above the scoreboard because the Room loves her work. — *Designed the lapel pins. — *Is the reason Hexfield looks like a sport instead of like eight people in a room. — *Visual director, for the record. Thank you.*"

He turned to Mei beside him. The bond hum at his sternum went *bright.*

"*Mei Chang. — *Cofounder. — *My pair-bonded cofounder.*"

He let that sit one beat.

"*Did the rosters with Imogen, drafted the bracket, did the math on a hundred and twenty of you so you all have a seat or a match or both, has been the spine of the day. — *And — *cast the patronus that opened this thing in the dark. Thank you.*"

He turned to the teachers' box.

"*Professor Henshaw — *for coming to look at a student-invented sport on a Saturday afternoon and for not stopping us from inventing it. — *Professor Sprout for the biscuits — *which I have not had yet but I can see them from here. — *Professor Kettleburn for the across-the-pitch finger-point at me when you came in which I have been carrying with me for fourteen minutes. — *Professor Sage and Madam Hooch for showing up to evaluate, which I welcome. Thank you all.*"

He turned to the announcer box.

"*The Hogwarts kitchens. — *Hippy, Tippy, Marble, Nessa — *and Bramble and Wisp and Pim and the under-elves who could not be here. — *Brought us the food. — *Then brought us — *more food. — *Are also in the announcer box because I asked them, and they came. — *Everyone — *please go eat the food. — *It is at the table on the north wall. — *It is the kitchens, of Hogwarts, in finger form.*"

A small reedy laugh from Tippy's riser, properly carrying. Hippy's pencil scratched at the clipboard.

"*Liri Penrose. — *Announcer-in-training-of-twenty-four-hours. — *Worked out the cadence, ran the lights cue, is about to do the announcing while I play the third match. — *Could not have done this without you. Thank you.*"

A breath. He looked across the hall — at Davina at her seat with the proper prefect-stillness, at Aisling and Mairi clapping properly from the Gryffindor benches, at Cass on the pitch beside him not making a face about it, at Esme Holroyd watching from the east benches with her sister's name in her chest, at the leopard cat lapel pin at Mei's collar.

"*That's the thank-you part. — *Now — *questions. — *Comments. — *Anyone want to jump in for the proper matches when we call your name? — *Liri — *first match — *take it.*"

He held the wand at his throat one more beat, lowered it, and stepped back to give Liri the radio.

▼ Saturday, 10 September 1977 — 14:07

Liri caught it on the first beat. Tilted her own wand-tip toward her throat. The Room registered the two-announcer-channel and split the radio's amplification so both voices carried equally.

"*Hexfield. — *Match Two. — *Six on six. — *Liri Penrose at the radio, Caleb Waddell beside her not knowing his place. — *Caleb. — *Call the teams.*"

Caleb leaned in.

Liri did not break.

"*The crowd is already on my side, Waddell. — *Read the room.*"

The hall laughed — properly. A clean four-house wave.

## Narrator

▼ Saturday, 10 September 1977 — 14:07

Caleb caught the whistle before Liri blew it — the small *hold on one second* hand-lift.

"*Liri — *one more thing for the crowd before we go.*"

Liri did not blow the whistle. Lowered it from her mouth properly. The radio re-amplified Caleb's voice at full carry.

"*One last rule — *and it is the *best one. — *Hexfield is played in the Room of Requirement. — *Most of you know what the Room of Requirement does. — *Some of you do not yet, and you are about to find out.*"

He paced one slow step across the announcer-box platform.

"*During play, the Room sometimes — *adds things. — *On its own. — *No one asked for them. — *We do not know what they will be. — *In our inaugural match on Sunday — *eight of us, the same eight you just watched — *the Room dropped an ankle-deep pond at centre-left mid-round, rotated a column ninety degrees in another, and pushed a cold draft through the third that smelled like the Forbidden Forest in November. — *None of which were on the floor plan.*"

A pause. The hall was listening properly.

"*The Room is — *not refereeing. — *It is — *playing too. — *It is — *a third team that doesn't care who wins. — *And it adds effects when the play gets interesting, which means — *the better you play, the weirder the pitch will get.*"

Liri picked it up beside him, smooth:

"*The effects are never hostile to a specific player. — *The Room is neutral. — *Effects fade when the Room loses interest, usually within a round. — *They are always playable-around — *no one is going to get bitten by a thing that materialises in their flag-run. — *But they can — *change the shape of a match. — *Plan around them as they appear, not before.*"

Caleb:

"*If a goat shows up on the pitch we deal with the goat. — *If the ceiling goes purple for thirty seconds, that is — *Saturday afternoon at Hexfield, eat your sandwich. — *If a fog rolls in, you keep playing in the fog. — *The Room makes its own calls. — *We work around them.*"

He raised his wand.

"*Now — *the whistle. — *Liri — *take the count.*"

## User

*jump ahead to an effect*

## Narrator

▼ Saturday, 10 September 1977 — 14:14

Match Two was eight minutes in.

Round Two — score still tied, no flag captures, Gold had taken three medic-revives and Purple had taken two. Imogen had pulled Lin out of a Knockback in fourteen seconds flat by going to her knees on the pitch in a way that drew applause from the watcher benches before Lin was even up. Cass — wait, Cass was watching Match Two from the cracker bowl, queued for Match Three — *Niamh Boyle* was the Purple defender, and Niamh had just trip-jinxed Toby on a flag-run that had been ninety percent of the way home.

Toby went down at the centre-pitch line in a bright gold glow, swearing colourfully, and Sorcha Mullaney started toward him at a run.

That was when the Room *added a thing.*

A circle of pitch about eight feet across — directly between Sorcha and Toby — *bloomed.*

Out of the soft inflatable turf came orange poppies and blue cornflowers and white ox-eye daisies and yellow buttercups, all knee-high in under three seconds, fully open as if it had been a sunny July morning all week. A faint scent of warm meadow rolled out of the circle and across the front three rows of the watcher benches; a fourth-year on the east side audibly said *oh.*

Sorcha stopped at the edge of the wildflower circle.

Toby, on the ground inside it, lifted his head, registered the flowers around him, and started laughing into his gold glow.

Caleb at the radio:

"*WHAT — *Liri — *I am sorry — *that is — *wildflowers. — *A meadow. — *In the middle of my sport. — *We have — *a literal flower-bed between Sorcha and her downed teammate. — *Penrose — *take it.*"

Liri, smooth:

"*The Room of Requirement has — *bloomed. — *Knee-high wildflowers, eight-foot diameter, smells appropriately of July, manifested in approximately three seconds. — *Tactical implications — *Sorcha can either wade through the flowers to reach Toby — *fragrant but slow, and Niamh can pick her off the moment she enters — *or go around the long way and burn her own thirty-second window getting there. — *Toby is — *laughing. — *Toby is genuinely laughing on the pitch with his timer counting. — *Sport.*"

The crowd was *delighted* — properly laughing, applauding the Room. The four house-elves in the announcer box had all stood up to look at the meadow. Hippy's pencil was scratching furiously at the clipboard.

Sorcha made the call. Waded into the flowers at a sprint. Niamh, from the Purple defensive line, fired a Stinging hex through the meadow toward her — and the hex hit a poppy mid-flight. The poppy flared bright red, took the hex, and *the hex stopped.* Niamh's mouth opened slightly.

Caleb on the radio:

"*The — *flowers — *are absorbing — *the hex. — *Penrose — *did the Room just invent a defensive ward.*"

Liri:

"*I believe the Room has — *added cover. — *Sorcha is now sprinting through wildflower cover toward Toby, and Niamh's hexes are — *being eaten by the petals. — *Imogen at the side-wall has stopped breathing.*"

Sorcha reached Toby. Dropped to a knee. Medic-touch initiated. Niamh shouted across the pitch to Donovan Tweed to *get round the meadow,* and Donovan was already moving.

The fragrance held across the hall. The mascot banners above the scoreboard had paused — the leopard cat sat back on her haunches looking down at the flower-circle, and the pygmy owl turned her head all the way around to track the change. Even the Room's elaborations were reacting to the Room's elaborations.

Mei, on the Gold defensive line, glanced once across the pitch at Caleb in the announcer box. The bond hum went *bright-amused.* Half-millimetre on.

## Narrator

▼ Saturday, 10 September 1977 — 14:17

Caleb at the radio, wand at his throat, full volume, looked directly at Mei across the hall as she lifted the water-flask to her mouth:

"*What do you mean you're pregnant?!*"

The hall *froze* on a clean half-second.

Mei lowered the water-flask. Did not change her expression. Did not flinch. Looked at him properly across the pitch with the half-millimetre on holding steady.

The room — properly silent for the count of *two whole beats* — leaned forward in its seats.

Then Caleb again, into the silence, the timing immaculate:

"*I'm just kidding. — *I'm sterile after a horrible microwaving accident.*"

The room broke in two.

The Muggle-borns and the half-bloods and the Muggle-aware *howled.* Liri at the radio doubled over visibly, the cornflower behind her ear shaking. Marlene McKinnon in the watcher benches was clapping with both hands above her head. Tien Nguyen had to sit down. A fifth-year Gryffindor Caleb did not know fell sideways into the person beside her.

The pure-bloods and the wizard-raised looked around at each other with the proper *what is a microwave* face on for the count of three before deciding to laugh anyway because everyone else was laughing.

Cass at the Purple bench had one hand over her mouth — the *I do not understand what he just said but I am here for the commitment* register.

Madam Hooch in the teachers' box: *bark* of laughter, single, surprised out of her. Professor Sage with a small smile. Sprout flat. Kettleburn properly snorted.

Henshaw — who had been raised in a half-blood household, the cohort had long since concluded — turned a page in his notebook, did not look up, and wrote one line.

Mei across the pitch, water-flask back at her lip, swallowed properly, lowered it, and used a single index finger to point at Caleb.

The bond hum at his sternum came through *loud:* *Caleb. You absolute disaster. I am writing this down. The cabin tonight is going to be EXCITING.*

Hippy in the announcer box riser was scratching furiously at her clipboard. Tippy — who would not know a microwave from a Floo grate — leaned over and whispered *what is a microwave* to Marble; Marble shook her head; both elves went back to laughing at the room laughing.

▼ Saturday, 10 September 1977 — 14:17

Caleb at the radio, the explanation flowing the same way the joke had — committed, half-grinning, the version of himself that did not quite stop to check whether he was making sense:

"*Jesus — *I'm not sure I know what a microwave is to explain it. — *It is like a Muggle box that heats food with like radio waves. — *The waves make the water in the food shake and that heats it up. — *It is like a fast stove.*"
